The Philippine Veterans.
General R. H. "VVarfleld, chairman of
the parade committee, has, with his com
mittee, taken steps to have the soldiers
now in San Francisco recently returned
from the Philippines and also those who
are due to return within the next few
days from the southern seas take part in
the parade. There are now In San Fran
cisco 9000 veterans who have marched un
der Uncle Sam's banner In the Philip
pines, who are about to be discharged.
Undoubtedly there are thousands of them
vrho have never seen President McKInley.
Dispatches have been sent to Secretary
of War Root and Adjutant General Cor
bin, asking them to hold the troops here
until after the arrival of the President,
that they may add their presence to the
occasion. Congressman Kahn has tele
graphed to "Washington in reference to
this matter and other Influences are at
work in the same direction. The parade
committee will meet next Saturday fore
noon at the office of Irving M. Scott and
there and then will discuss its plans. The
procession will be purely military, em
bracing the Philippine veterans and other
regular troops at the Presidio and the
Second Brigade of the National Guard of
California. These will make an imposing
and highly interesting pageant.

SEATTLE, April 22}.â Gold in a wolfs
hide is the subject of the latest story from
the wilds of Alaska and it is given out by
Captain H. F. DIxon, an old Yukon River
pilot,' who Is now visiting friends in Seat
tle. He says that he knows the story
to be true because he killed the wolf and
saw the gold himself. It consisted of fine
dust and a few small . nuggets, the total
value. being several dollars..
How the gold got Into the wolfs hide
has caused no end of speculation and
prospectors are searching the ground near
where the wolf was killed in the hope of
finding Its lair. It is supposed that the
gold came from dirt against which the
animal had been rubbing:,

Special Dispatch to The Call.
STOCKTON, April 22.-Thirteen-year
old James Morgan to-day confessed to
having set fire to the house of his par
ents, a half mile out of Lodi, last night.
The Morgan hcuse was fired last Thurs
day night the first time, while five chil
dren of the family were in the house
and the mother was in Lodi. Young
Morgan denies having started the first
fire, but his confession of the attempt to
burn the dwelling last evening leaves no
doubt In the mind3 of the officers that ho
alone is guilty. Three children were
sleeping over the place where the blaze
was set. Only hard work on the part of
neighbors saved their lives and the prop
erty.
Deputy Sheriff "Wall was sent to Investi
gate. Young Morgan confessed to having
started the second fire and said when
questioned: -
"My father Is preaching down In Bak
ersfleld and I don't want to stay in Lod!,
as the boys there torment me. I thought
that if I burned the house down my
mother would move away."
The Morgans are a colored family, con
sisting of father, mother and eight chil
dren, of which the youthful prisoner la
next to the oldest.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, April 22.â The celebrated
voyage around the world by Captain Jo
siah Slocum in his forty-foot sloop Daunt
less has been matched by the wedding
trip of Roy Ovaett, a lad of 19. Single
handed, Ovaett sailed an eighteen-foot
sloop from Seattle to Seward City, which
is near Juneau, Alaska. Then he put
about and beat his way home again, ar
riving here less than a week ago. Captain
Slocum sailed with a fully provisioned and
thoroughly equipped boat. Roy Ovaett
had scanty supplies, $10 in money,, not a
stick of spare canvas nor a foot of extra
spars and a young wife to care for. That
in'brief is the story. Told in detail the
narrative would make a book.
Ovaett is a modest young chap, with
clear blue eyes and a frank smile. His
wife is a beautiful young woman. She
was Meg Goodell before her marriage, and
the long months at sea have only added
to the charm of her face.
Sailed Away Last July.
The young couple were married early
in last July andÂ«fl. few days later the hus
band went to Frank Faber, who owns the
Brighton boathouse, and rented a sloop
named the Ultra. He told , Faber he was
going for a wedding trip, and the sloop
was provisioned for a two weeks' cruise.
The Ultra left port on the 28th of . last
July. Three weeks later Faber received a
letter from Allard Bay, in which Ovaett
'announced* thai^he-was sroing-to/take' his
wife to Alaska, and that 'he was not com
ing back.
From the day of her departure the Ultra
was not spoken by any craft. She disap
peared as completely as if she had sunk
to the bottom of the Pacific. And that is
what in time people came to believe was
her fate. ; â¢
' Mrs. Ovaett. nrlor to her marriage, had
lived with her grandmother, Mrs. Goodell,
in a little cottage close by the water front.
Mrs. Goodell gave up her grandchild as
dead and young .Ovaett's family mourned
for him as one lost to them forever.
Return of the Ultra.
A week ago the Ultra sailed jauntily up
the bay and dropped anchor off the Brigh
ton boathouse. Many things happened
during the long voyage to Alaska. There
were storms and head winds. Provisions
ran shortâ perilously short at times. Once
they lived for two days on a handful of
clams. On another occasion the Ultra
staggered along in the teeth of a gale un
til her trysail parted, and for thirty-nine
hours this boy stood at the tiller, holding
her head close to the wind. Had the Ultra
fallen off she would have upset.
Sitting in his cozy home this afternoon
Ovaett told the story of his wonderful
cruise along the shore of the North Pacific
to Seward, Alaska; After working his
way out _ into the ocean from Pug-et
Sound he took what is known as the inner
passage to Alaska. That he was not
wrecked a dozen times was due to vigi
lance and the numerous sheltered nooks
along the irregular passage.
Venison for Their I/arder. *
His two weeks' provisions were made to
cover a much longer period by the prac
tice of economy. and the addition of fish
and deer meat. Ovaett had no trouble ob
taining plenty of fish and when the meat
larder ran low he resorted to the old fash
ioned plan of "jacking" -for deer.. There
are many places along the coast even how
where deer may be killed with compara
tive ease. Ovaett had a gun with him,
and owing" to the necessity of making each
shot tell, got good results.
The sloop touched at Allard bay, from
which point Mrs. Ovaett was sent home
by steamer, while Ovaett sailed" home
without assistance. Â¦ - '

ers already budding will not Buffer from
the storm. A. work seriously delayed by
the snow la the laying of asphalt pave

be delayed to some extent because of the
deep mud. which makes transportation of
heavy displays in. the grounds practically
impossible. ,

CINCINNATI, April 22.â Alarm over tha
flood has almost ceased in the lower Ohio
valley. The low water mark in 'the Ohio
below this city has' allowed the floods
from up the river to flow rapidly toward
the Mississippi. The situation is still
serious up . the river,: notably at Rlpley,
Marysville, Aberdeen, Portsmouth. Hang-
Ing Rock, Ironton, Ashland, CatUetts
burg, Huntington, Point Pleasant, Pome
roy, Marietta, Parkersburg and some
other places, between Wheeling and Cin
cinnati. The crest of the flood is below
Wheeling to-night and the further down
stream it gets the lower Is the rate of
risjng. . Â¦ Â¦
The uniform rate of three Inches per
hour this morning fell to two Inches be
fore noon and as low as one and a half
inches an -hour at Point Pleasant at 2 p.
m: This decrease in the rate did not ex
tend far below Point Pleasant, so that it
will: be another day before it reaches
Portmouth. Ironton. Ashland, Cattletts
burg and Huntington, where there aro al
ready serious conditions. It usually takes
the crest of the flood two days to reach
Cincinnati from Point Pleasant, but the
present 'unobstructed flow is much more
rapid than in former floods. At Ports
mouth to-night fifty-five, feet was reported
and that means serious conditions at
points below Portsmouth. The Weather
Bureau indicates that the. flood will not
exceed fifty-tight feet at any time at
Cincinnati, and possibly it may not reach
fifty-seven feet. It is over fifty-two feet
to-night and the maximum will not be
reached before to-morrow night or Wed
nesday. This is about ten feet less than
was anticipated here until the river be
gan falling last night at Pittsburg.
Not as Bad as Former Floods.
The flood is not' as bad as In a^uozen
former years and much below the record
of 1897 and 1898, and those of 1S83 and 1S84.
The former floods were earlier, when the
weather was cold, causing much distress
among the homeless people. While this
flood is not so hard on the people driven
from their homes, it is much harder on the
crops and fruits that are well advanced.
The property loss in the rural districts
cannot be estimated. It will be very large.
The homeless people will not be kept
away from their houses as long as they
were during former floods, which in some
cases have lasted as many weeks in the
winter as this will continue days in the
spring. The highest stage will be reached
at Parkersburg to-morrow, and here the.
next day and at the mouth of the Ohio
before the week is out.
The Weather Bureau here to-night
gave out the statement that the Ohio
River in the Cincinnati district generally
was above the danger line from one foot
at Cincinnati to fourteen feet at Point
Pleasant and rising at an average of two
inches an hour. It indicates that the rise
would continue at Cincinnati until Wed
nesday, when the river will become sta
tionary and soon begin falling. The
Bureau predicts fifty- four feet here to
morrow moraine and fifty-six to-morrow
evening.
Heavy Losses Are Sustained.
The most encouraging Information re
ceived by the Weather Bureau was from
Parkersburg. where there was a rise of
only one foot all day and a stage of only
forty-two feet was recorded where the
local river men had anticipated forty
eight. It. was expected that >the river
would begin falling at Parkersburg to
morrow. At Point Pleasant, another criti
cal place farther down the river, the
water was rising much more slowly.
At Pomeroy. which is located In a great
bend, the suffering to-night Is reported
less than in former floods. The large
furnaces of the salt trust are located here,
and they have suffered much loss, aÂ»
well as the lumbermen, the electric in
ter-urban lines, the light and other cor
porations. - Similar conditions exist at
MInersville, but all the points In the
Pomeroy Bend have escaped. The reports
from other up-river points that suffered
so severely during other floods indicate
that no outside help is needed, that there
has been no loss of life except in' acci
dental drownlngs from shanty boats and
that there is no distress for want of food
or shelter.
HIGH WATER IMPEDES TRAFFIC
Is Done Along the Ohio.
At Cincinnati Considerable Damage
CINC1NNATT, April 23.â The stage of
the river here at 6 o'clock to-night was
fifty-one feet and rising at the rate of two
inches an hour. This means fifty-three
feet at 6 o'clock to-morrow morning, when
trains will be shut off from the Grand
Central station. The roads using that sta
tion have already arranged to use the
Fourth and Eighth street stations to-mor
row. All the railways have also made ar
rangements for receiving and delivering
freight at higher stations so that there
will be no Interruption here either to pas
senger or freight traffic. Owing to the in
ability of the eteamers to get under the
bridges, navigation on the river is sus
pended, but the predicted subsidence of
the flood will probably permit a resump
tion of river traffic by Thursday.
Owing to washouts, the Cincinnati.
Portsmouth and Virginia Railway cannot
run trains.
The residents of the tenement houses
along the Ohio River front were run out
of the first storJes when the river reached
a stage of forty-five feet last night. "What
Is known as the danger line of fifty feet
was not reached until 1 o'clock to-day,
since which time the jobbers, warehouses
and manufacturers in the lower part of
the city have been suffering. The flooding
of basements wJll compel many manufac-
turers to shut down for two or three d-*ys.
The river here was rising at the rate of
three inches an hour this moning. The
flood is unobstructed In tne lower Ohio, and
there Is less backwater, than usual, which
conditions give hope that the trouble will
be of short duration. It 13 settled now that
if a second rise from the Allegheny re
gion comes it will not overtake the pres
ent flow. So far the damage here has
been limited to the lumbermen and others
located along the river front.
Mayor Fleischmann. Chief of Police
Deitsch, Health Officer Davis and other9
inspected the flooded district to-day in
patrol wagons and boats, and arranged for
the relief of all who were in need. An ex
tra police was put on In the flooded dis
tricts, and the police boat patrols will be
on the river front to-morrow.
The endless lino of shanty boats and
other craft along the river front have
been swept away, but so far without loss
The " country along 1 thÂ© Little Miami
River is badly flooded Just east of the
city.
At Newport and Dayton. Ky.. many peo
ple have been driven from their homes.
The waterworks of Newport are unable
to do pumping, but the reservoir is full
and no trouble -is expected during the
short duration of the flood. It is now set
tled that the Newport race traÂ£k -Â«|llnot
be flooded. Tho races are proceeding^crV*
der very sloppy conditions.
At Huntingtcn. W. Va., to-night the
Ohio is 64.3 and rising an inch an hour.

WATERS ARE RECEDING.
Damage at Plttsburg and Vicinity
"Will Amount to Two "Million
Dollars.
PITTSBURG, April 22.â The flood la
over. Notwithstanding tho fact that early
In the day there was every evidence of a
renewal of the high water, the announce
ment can be made authoritatively to
night that there is not the least danger
of another rise at this point nor above
for the present, but towns on the Ohio
River below here are not yet out of dan
ger because of rains in tha valley last
nicht and to-day.
All up-river points report the rain as
having ceased and the river falling 1 . ThÂ©
Ohio registered 19.8 at the dam here at
7 o'clock and was falling. Forecaster
Ridgeway says It would require a steady
downpour of rain for twenty-four hour3
to check the fall, and from present Indi
cations such a thing Is not ukely.
The rivers about this city are down so
that the works along- the banks are run
ning. There is a good deal of cleaning
up to be done. The flood brought in much
slimy mud that it left when it receded,
and it will reaulra a day of two to re
move it from many of the mills and clean,
tho machinery.
The creeks and runs -which did so much
damage in the outlying districts on Satur
day are back well wltoln their banks to
night. Carnegie Is rapidly cleaning up
and repairing Dridges, houses and roads.
The same state of affairs exists at Mo
Kees, Recks and other towns that tha
water reached.
Â¦ The railroads havÂ« recovered remark
ably Well from the effects of the flood
and snowstorm, and the various lines
flave resumed operations to all points,
but for several days no pretension at run
ning on schedule time will be made.
A look over the ground "after the flood
shows that the losses will be fully as
great as was estimated yesterday. In
and about Plttsburg It will probably take
$1,000,000 to pay the total cost of the injury
to property by the water and landslides.
Add to this the great loss to thÂ© rail
roads and manufacturing' plants up and
down the rivers in their immediate- vicin
ity, with the loss In -wages to> the work
ingmen, and tho total will not fall rax
short Of $2,000,000. i ---

AT.ABAMA WILL VOTE
ON CONSTITUTION TO-DAY
Advocates of Measure Eliminating
Negro Vote by Educational Qual-
ification Are Confident.
BIRMINGHAM, Ala., April 22.â With
stump speeches in nearly every county in
the State, the campaign for a constitu
tional convention closed to-day. A gen
eral election will be held to-morrow, and
indications point to an overwhelming vic
tory for the advocates of the new con
stitution.
The principal change, and practically
the only change to be made in the con
stitution, is the elimination of the negro
vote by educational qualification. On this
point the political contest has been
fought, and there is organized opposition
to the new constitution In some of the
counties. The Democrats who are behind
the movement for radical change in suf
frage laws, feel confident of success. The
Democratic party has pledged itself, to
see that no white man will -be disfran
chised under the constitution which they
hope to have adopted.

NEW YORK, April 22.â The directors of
the Missouri Pacific Railroad at a meeting
to-day authorized an increase of $30,000,000
in the capital stock, making the total
authorized $100,000,000. A stockholders'
meeting will be held shortly to ratify this
action.
It was reported that a large part of the
new-st&ck would be available for taking
up Denver and Rio Grande preferred and
common shares. Information as to the
price of issue or other details could not
be obtained from the company's offices.
The Missouri Pacific's present outstand
ing capital is $50,432,000. The authorize'";
amount was increased to $70,000,000 about*
a year ago, but it was then stated that
the increase was made because Missouri
,laws required a certain ratio between the
bonds and stocks, of corporations to be
maintained. The new stock then author
ized has not been issued up to the present
time. . Â¦-. , ... 4
The Denver and Rio Grande stock out
standing is $30,000,000 common and $23,650,
000 preferred. Tha latter issue, however,
will be increased to $44,000,000 at the spe
cial shareholders' meeting called for May
15 to provide for the Rio Grande Western
purchase. Of this new stock $12,500,000 Is
to be issued at once for cash, for the Rio
Grande Western common shares, and pre
sumably the greater share of the amount
reserved will be held for exchanging Rio
Grande Western preferred stock. If the
Missouri Pacific proposed to completely
absorb the Denver and Rio Grande Rail
road, therefore, the greater part of the
unissued stock would be required" for that
purpose.

MORE MILLIONS
I N A RAILROAD
MEMPHIS, Tenn.. April 22.â The fast
express train of the Choctaw. Oklahoma
and Gulf Railroad, which left Memphis
at 11:40 o'clock to-night, was held up t>y
three masked bandits at Bridge Junction,
Ark., about midnight. It is not known
Â¦what booty the robbers secured, but a dis
patch received at police headquarters
stated that the express messenger .and
porter of the train were injured after re
sisting the bandits. The Wells-Fargo Ex
press Company usually makes its heaviest
shipments to the West on this train. Po
lice Sergeant Perry, upon receipt of the
telegram, Immediately posted officers
along the river front with instructions to
keep a sharp lookout for the bandits,
should they attempt to cross to the city. '
The train left Memphis with a heavy
passenger list. The scene of the hold-up
is a lonely railroad crossing about four
miles from the river. It is said that the
negro porter was shot by one of the ban
dits aod Is in a serious condition.
The Train was in charge of Conductor
Nelson, one of the oldest employes of the
road, and Engineer Johnston. The ex
press messenger. Meadows, is said to have
been shot, but It cannot be learned if any
valuables were secured. The negro por
ter, Gould, was shot and is in a critical
condition. 'The train was delayed about
twenty minutes.
Bloodhounds from the convict camp at
Hulbert, three miles from the scene, are
now on the bandits' trail.
The engine, mail and " express car ' were
cut off from the train and run to a point
half a mile west of the place where the
train was stopped. The engine was then
detached and run a short distance up the
track, two men remaining cuard over the
trainmen. The third used dynamite on
the exoress car and blew open the doors.
It is reported that everything of value in
the express car was taken and it is be
lieved that the bandits' haul is large, as
this was a. heavy run.

City Attorney Booth's , address of wel
come was in part as follows:
It seems to me that it Is peculiarly appro
priate that Santa Barbara should have been
chosen by you as a gathering: place. , As a
village in early days, it played no Inconspicu
ous part In the making of California history.
Our State, more especially the southland, is
thoroughly cosmopolitan; It Is now peopled by
men .and. women most' of whom have owed their
allegiance to other sovereign States. And in
the haste of to-day we, the later dwellers, are
apt to forget, the struggles of those pioneers
who. made it possible for us.to/now enjoy the
blessings of a stable government, a fruitful
land, a rich commonwealth. It' is, due. to their
memory that from among their sons and
daughters spring up. fraternal orders
whose members have the common heritage
of pluck and endurance that carved from an
unknown land the imperial State of California.
Tribute to the Pioneers.
Reflect but a moment on the deeds of the
pioneers and one must exclaim, "Ah, my mas
ters, there were men!" Hen who made the
wildernets blossom as the rose; who wrested
from stubborn nature the secrets of prosperity;
who turned over the virgin soil to see spring
from it fragrant orchards and waving grains:
who compelled the "gray and melancholy"
waste of ocean to ray tribute to them, the
stern and forbidding mountains to reveal hid
den riches surpassing those of fabled Golconda.
To the sons and daughters of these pioneers
It is my privilesre on behalf of the city of
Santa Barbara to extend a welcome heartfelt
and a genuine "last toast and of obligationâ a
health to the native born." ,

WILLIAM FEARS
AN ANARCHIST
PEKING. April 22.-General Voyron, the
commander of the French troops in China,
has Informed General Chaffee that 10,000
French soldiers will leave China In May.
Li Hung Chang believeB that the Chinese
troops under General Liu will be with
drawn over the boundary marklnj? the
territory defined by Field Marshal von
TValdersee as the sphere of operations for
the allies, as the Governor of Shansi prov
ince received telegraphic instructions
nearly a week ago ordering their with
drawal. The Governor could have con
veyed this order to the troops in question
by last Friday at the latest. Â¦
The majority of the French and German
newspaper correspondents accompanying
the expedition directed against General
Liu. and which has been mobilized at
Paotlngfu, returned to Peking to-day, be
lieving that the expedition would be called
off.
Hsi Liang, formerly Governor of the
province of Shansi, has been appointed
Governor of the province of Hupei. The
foreign consuls at Hankow, capital of the
province of Hupei, have protested against
this appointment to Chang Chi Tung,
Viceroy of Hankow, and have sent a tele
gram advising him to delay proceeding in
the matter of Hsl Liang's appointment.
Civilians Are Alarmed.
The numerous appointments of China
men with pronounced anti-foreign ten
dencies is creating comment at Peking.
Even the foreign Ministers admit that so
many appointments of this character are
ill-advised, while the missionaries and
other civilians are alarmed for the future.
It Is the opinion of Mr. Rockhill. the
American special commissioner, that the
foreign troops may now commence leav-
Inj? China with perfect safety. . On the
other hand the announcement that 10,000
French troops are to leave in May causes
fear among the French native converts
and French residents.
The views of the foreign Ministers ..on the
withdrawal of the troops are not shared
by the great majority of the foreign resi
dents. The Ministers aver that some of
these latter have business reasons and
others would have to vacate the line
buildings they are now occupying rent
free when the troops go.
Would Protect the General.
A personage from Sianfu, who Is closely
connected with the court, denies that Gen
eral Tung Fuh Siang is in a state of open
rebellion. The person in question thinks
that the missionaries who reported that
he was In rebellion must have heard ac
counts of his harsh treatment of bandits
falling into his hands. He has frequently
disobeyed orders of the court. When 5000
troops were withdrawn from his com
mand last January he should have re
turned their rifles and ammunition. This
he failed to do. Before he left for Kansu
he obtained an audience with the Em
press. Upon this occasion he said to the
Km press:
"All that your servant has done has
been to obey the orders of the court. He
will die upon the battlefield in your ser
vice. If, because of the commands of the
foreigners, your servant were committed
to death he would die unwillingly."
To this the Empress replied
"Let your heart be at peace. I would
rather lose my rivers and mountains than
you. I will surely devise a plan for your
protection."
Court Advisers Not in Accord.
The same informant says that the court
advisers are not in accord.
Lu Chuan Lin. a notorious reactionary
and president of the Board of Trade,
argues in favor of subduing the barbari
ans, while others advise delay in return
ing to Peking. Only Weng Weng Shao
and Sun Chian Nai, urge immediate ac-:
ceptance of the terms offered privately.
Two of the foreign powers have separate
ly agreed to extend to the court the fuil
protection, should necessity arise, of
every soldier they have.
At a recent meeting of the council both
the Emperor and the Empress wept fre
quently. The Dowager Empress said that
each day of absence was a day when ehe
could neither eat nor sleep in peace. The
Emperor said that Peking was the home
of his ancestors and that he could not
bear to abandon it.
i Sir Robert Hart's plan for the in
crease of the revenue of China would
bring in 20,000,000 taels annually, but the
foreigners and business men of China ob
ject to this plan, saying that under it
the foreign business houses will pay the
greater portion of the indemnity.

LONDON, April 21â According to a dis

guns.

nese under the former" Governor of Muk
den. To the eastward in Mongolia and
near the In-Shan mountain there are 900^
more under the Chinese General Schu.
The situation in southern Manchuria- Is
disquieting ' and another advance 'pf Rus
sian troops will be made in -the early

I 7I8 Market Street. IMANILA, April 22.â A representative of
the Associated Press visited .Kmilio
Aguinaldo this afternoon at 56 Solano
street, where he was removed from the
Malacanan Palace, and found him in a
large room upstairs, furnished with a table
typewriting machine, four settees and
twenty chairs. His wife, who was enter
taining a number of Filipino women
friends, sat at one end of the room, while
Aguinaldo,- smoking a cigar and chatting
with Benlto Legardo, occupied the oppo
site corner.
- Others present were Lieutenant Colonel
John S. Mallory of the Forty-first Volun
teer Infantry, who has charge of Aguin
aldo; Lieutenant Gilbert A. Youngberg of
the Third Artillery and Mr. Fisher, Gen
eral MacArthur*s private secretary. !
Â¦Aguinaldo, whose bearing was courteous
and dignified, was dressed in white, looked
well and altogether made an excellent Im
pression. - Legardo, who but recently re
turned from the United States, talked of
the - trip, and Aguinaldo, who seemed
greatly interested, smiled and asked nu
merous questions. He inquired particu
larly as to what President McKinley said
and seemed anxious, to know -what Â¦was
thought of him in the United States.
He was rather reluctant to talk for pub
lication and considered every question
particularly before answering. He said
he was doing- all he could to assist in the
pacification of the Filipinos and expressed
himself as surprised at what the Amer
icans had accomplished. When he was
first captured, he went on to say, he was
greatly astonished to find that a majority
of the Filipinos entertained the opinion
that American sovereignty was preferable
to independence, but now he was inclined
to believe that way ! himself.

Stampedes.
SEATTLE, April 22.-From Valdes un
der date of April 6, comes reports that the
first party to reach that place this win
ter from the Yukon over the All-Ameri
can route arrived there on March 31. The
party consisted of S. James, Richard
Stewart, Paul Paulson and Carl Ramstad
They made the trip from Forty-Mile to
Valdes in sixteen days of actual travel
The trail was reported to have been In
good condition, with large numbers of
people passing. ,
On Forty-Mile great activity is report
ed and a recent strike made on the head
waters on Walker's Fork, a tributary of
the South Fork of Forty-Mile, caused a
stampede thither some time 'ago. Many
men are leaving the Yukon camps for the
Chestochlna, which seems to be a favor
ite place this season for mining opera
tions. Late reports from Prince Williams
Sound are very encouraging as to thÂ»
mining outlook 1n that district.
There is considerable activity In the
Copper River Valley. Recent reports from
there say that representatives of the
great copper companies of the United
States, including one of Senator W A
Clark's men from Montana, are on' tfie
ground locating and bonding properties
A large number of horses and outfits are
being taken into the country.
Since the beginning of the town of
Valdes . only . five deaths have occurred
one of which was an accidental drowning.'

AXL -AMERICAN ROUTE
The American forces had a hard time
compelling the Panay Island natives to
surrender, and finally orders camo to de
stroy everything. Keegan says a dog
could not have lived on the island after
our forces finished the work of desolation
\vhen the island had been made barren
the natives surrendered. Keegan believes
the United States will be compelled to
carry out the Spanish custom of garrison
lnr- the small towns?, owing to the treach
erous character of the natives.
The Twenty-sixth was stationed at
Iloilo, island of Panay, and it was here
that Maurice first saw bullets sped at
human targets. He Is the only Califor
nian in the regiment, most of the men in
the Twenty-sixth being of Massachusetts
blood. The regiment went out with 1300
men and came back 900 strong. Only six
were killed in battle, but nearly 400 re
turned sick and wounded. The men ex
pect to be discharged in a few weeks.
Young Keegan tells of a number of
skirmishes and battles in which his regi
ment was engaged. In one battle the
American forces numbered 4000, while the
insurgents were strongly entrenched near
Jaro with 8000 men. They would not aim,
but raised their rifles over the trenches
and fired, the result being that their bul
lets went wide. Every insurgent found
dead in the trenches was shot in the fore
head, which proved that they did not ex
pose much of themselves. When the
Americans charged, the natives ran for
the tall grass.
SANTA ROSA, April 22.-Not yet 19
years of age, Maurice Keegan, son of ex-
Councllman Thomas P. Keegan of this
city, has served two years In Uncle Sam's
army in the Philippines and is now home
on a furlough. Maurice Keegan was a
student in a San Francisco school about
four years ago, when he suddenly decided
he would go East. Without informing his
parents in this city of his intentions he
went to Boston. When next heard from
young Keegan had enlisted 'in the Twen
ty-sixth Regiment, United States Volun
teers, in Boston. That was in July, 1809,
and with his regiment he left immediate
ly for the Philippines.

Superior Sunset companies over the own
ership of the land that caused the bloody
conflict of last Thursday night has been
amicably settled by representatives of the
companies, and in consequence the possi
bility of another clash between the Supe
rior Sunset forces and the Vigilantes is
now at an end. This afternoon a Superior
Sunset man, with full powers, approached
the Mount Diablo Company and made
overtures for peace and a settlement. The
proposition was promptly accepted by the
Mount Diablo people and word was at
once wired to McKittrick to send out a
courier to notify the officers of the Vigi
lantes that peafe had been proclaimed.
The Superior Sunset men soon afterward
started a fast rider to their laager in Mid
way with a similar inessase, but it was
so arrang-ed that he was to arrive there
after the Vigilantes had been informed of
the triumph of peace.
In brief, the Superior Sunset people ad
mitted that they were wrong in going
upon the land and asked that they be
leased eighty acres where their camp is
now located. They were given the lease
and will now. as soon as the instrument
is drawn, proceed to exploit the land in
peace.
It was agreed to dismiss the Injunction
proceedings instituted against the Mount
Diablo as soon as the lease was signed,
and as to the criminal prosecutions they,
of course, will be left to the authorities.
The trouble on section 25, the adjoining
land, has also been compromised in a sim
ilar way and all legal actions dismissed.
It is hrjped that these two settlements
â¢will end the Midway war. but there are
many who believe there is trouble yet to
come. Feeling against claim jumpers is
mounting to a high pressure and is cer
tain to bring on bloodshed should any fur
ther claim Jumping be attempted.
Walker and Cornell, the men wounded
In Thursday night's battle, are yet alive
and there is a possibility that they will
recover.

SACRAMENTO, April 22,-The arrival
of delegates to-day to the State Sunday
School Convention was conflned-to a few
persons having friends or relatives in this
city. The influx of visitors will not be
noticeable until to-morrow; and as,a con
sequence the labors of the reception com
mittee will not begin until the arrival of
the Folsom" and Placerville train at 9-33
in the morning. From that hour the
committee will be at .the railroad depot
to receive and welcome arrivals on each
incoming train, and its work will be ar
duous. There are fifty members of the
committee,, however, and the delegates
after their reception, will be turned over
to the conductor boys, who will escort
them to the lodgings prepared by the reg
istration committee. The reception com
mittee will wear white caps with a blue
band, bearing in gilt letters the word
"Reception." ,
At 2:55 p.m. the delegates from San
Jose, Stockton, Gait and the southern
counties will arrive, probably on a special
train, and it is expected that 500 or more
will be aboard. .
Every precaution for the comfort of the
visitors has been taken by the local com
mittees, and that all will be well cared
for Is certain.

GATHERING TO PLOT
AGAINST WICKEDNESS
SEATTLE, April 22.â John Williams, an
Alaska pioneer of 1SS2 and one of the best
known prospectors and miners of the
northern country, arrived in Juneau on
April 15 to give himself up to the Federal
authorities, making the confession that at
Glacier Bay on April 2 he had shot and
killed an Indian named Dick Dunn, after
a desperate encounter. The Indian was
well known In Juneau, where he had
borne a fairly good reputation and was
noted for his physical strength. The cir
cumstances leading: up to the affray in
which he was killed indicate, that Dick
Dunn, became suddenly insane and pos
sessed of a homicidal mania.
"Williams hired Dunn at- Juneau last
month to accompany him on a prospect
ing trip to the Glacier Bay district. The
pair left Juneau in a rowboat. A small
sail was hoisted, with the Indian steering
and "Williams seated in the bow of the
boat. When they had nearly reached their
destination Williams, whose back was to
ward the Indian, was startled by the re
port of a rifle and the whiz of a bullet
past his head. Turning In surprise he
Inquired of Dunn why he had shot, and
the Indian, pointing over the bow, said
he had seen a seal ahead and had taken
a shot at it. At the tinte Williams
thought nothinc of the occurrence.
Indian Sharpens His Knife.
They made camp on "the shore of Gla
cier Bay. In tha evening the Indian pre
ceded Williams in the tent and when "Wil
liams attempted to enter he was again
startled by seeing the rifle In the hands
of Dunn, pointed directly at him. Wil
liams save a well and started back from
the entrance, but the Indian again made
a ready excuse, saying he believed it was
some one else coming in.
The night passed without mishap and
during the next day the Indian sharpened
his knife, with the remark that It was not
sharp enough to cut .Williams' neck. This,
with other remarks, caused Williams some
uneasiness, and he could not sleep on re
tiring. After lying awake some time he
heard the click of the Indian's double
barreled shotgun. As he arose to a sit
ting position and inquired what was the
matter the Indian covered him and de
manded his rifle, saying that it was Wil
liams he was afraid of. ; '.
Realizing the meaninsr of the past ac
tions of the Indian, Williams swung his
rifle around and flred. The report of the
Indians' weapon and his own sounded at
the same time. Before he could rise the
Indian leaped upon him with knife in
hand.
Bullet Ends the Combat.
Williams, .who -is a smaller man. was
completely smothered by the heavy In
dian. For a time he was nearly helpless
and he no doubt would have had little
chance for his life had not his revolver
come to hand by the overturning of his
bedclothes. Calling vainly to the Indian
to desist and seeing no way to overcome
the maniac, he placed the revolver to
Dunn's breast and killed him. â¢
Early in the morning after the affray
Williams started on the return trip to Ju
neau.. He experienced considerable diffi
culty in managing the heavy boat against
head winds and among the strong cur
rents, and it was eleven days before he
reached his destination. He immediately
gave himself to the authorities and told
the details of the killing. It was not be
lieved in Juneau at the time the steam
ship Victorian.- which arrived here on
Sunday, left that port that any further

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 23, 1901.

The smallpox epidemic bids fair to be
come general among the Indians of
Southeastern Alaska. According to re
ports from the north by the steamship
Cottage City, arriving to-day, there are
eighty cases in the Sitka Indian village,
and as the Indians are stampeding in all
directions -in canoes, it is feared they will
carry the disease to other villages. Sltkn.
has been placed under quarantine and
guards have been placed at all places of
entrance and exit. While the Cottage
City was at Sitka her passengers were
.not allowed to land.

wrecked bank who yesterday called upon

were found to be in such shape that any
thing could be done, to comply with the
committee's request.

ST. LOUIS, April 22.â Nouroulah, the gi-
gantlc Bulgarian, gained an easy victory
over Charles Wittmer, the Cincinnati
heavyweight wrestler, at the Exposition
Coliseum to-night, winning two straight
falls in exactly five minutes. The match
was catch-as-catch-can, best two in three
falls, all holds to go. The European's im
mense bulk and science were altogether
too much for the Cincinnatian to, combat
successfully. In the first bout Nouroulah
back-heeled Wittmar and fell upon him
with his whole weight. He secured a
crotch hold, which he switched to a half
hitch and after a short stuggle pinned
both of Wlttmar's shoulders to the mat.
Time, 2:00.
Coming on for the second fall, Wittmer
secured a strangle hold, which the Bul
garian easily broke and then threw 'Witt
mer to 'the mat. Â¦ The Cincinnatian strug
gled out of half a dozen holds, but Nour
oulah finally encompassed him in a half-
Nelson and half-hammer, which gave him
the victory. Time, 3:00. Nouroulah tipped
the beam at 354 and Wittmer at 215 pounds.

to stomach and jaw. With a hard right. he

he almost fell out of the ring, going partly
through the ropes. McGovern would not be

gained his feet and was almost done for. The

Terry started with a rush In the third and

and right to the chin quickly. Oscar gave him

stood over him and tolled off the fateful ten
and it was all over. Time of round nineteen
seconds. Oscar's seconds carried him to his
corner, where he recovered after a short while.
Rush, for Seats.
. The sale of seats for the fight opened
yesterday and the rush gives ample proof
that Mechanics' Pavilion will be crowded
to the doorsÂ» on the night of the great

the champion cyclist, called' on. Manager

yesterday and reported that a large crowd

McGOVERN has changed his
training quarters. The little
whirlwind and his retinue of
trainers moved from the Casino
yesterday to the Terminal Ho
tel, corner Haight and Stanyan streets,
and now all those wishing to see the
champion in action will either have to
come prepared with "credentials or wait
until the night of his battle with Gardner.
Manager Harris has found that this "rule
must be made as the curious have come
in such numbers that McGovern and his
assistants have been practically crowded
out of their, gymnasium.
The new training quarters are perfectly
satisfactory to McGovern. The spacious
gymnasium, the business-like punching
bag platforms and muscle-making appar
atus pleased him greatly. He jolted the
bag a few times, tried the wrist machine
and smiled blandly. . . -. â¢
Dnkelpw Takes a Rest.
McGovern worked hard yesterday after
noon. He sparred with Dougherty and a
youth named Healy, punched the bag and
side-stepped around the room more than
an hour. Dukelow, the amateur welter
weight, was given a rest for the nonce
but McGovern warned him that he -would
warm him up te-day. Dukelow smiled
grimly and remarked that he was glad
to report that he had not lost one of his
gold teeth up to this time, but added that
he would not crow unil he was out of the
woods. ~v .- .
Sam Harris, manager of McGovern and
Dougherty, denied the statement that
Dougherty was down too fine. He says
that Danny has not started to train and
that he is heavier now than ever before in
his lifetime. Harris said that Dougherty
always looked drawn about the face, but
this 'was no indication that he was over
trained. He remarked that Dougherty
Â¦would enter the ring weighing 108 pounds
and in shape to give "Kid" McFadden the
hottest battle he ever had In his life.
Oscar Gardner is working like a Trojan
at Croll's Gardens, Alameda. He ex
presses great confidence in his ability to
defeat McGovern and says he will enter
the ring trained to the hour. He has wait
ed for this chance to retrieve his defeat
at the hands of McGovern and he vows
that he will make the most of it.

ner's first battle is interesting at this
time. Just a year ago they .battled before

York. Following is an excerpt from a de

writer:

Bsnny Yanger and Johnnie Ritchey
MEMPHIS, Tenn., . April ' ' 22.â Benny
Yanger, the "Tipton Slasher," of Chicago,
and Johnnie Ritchey of St. Louis, fought
twenty fast rounds to. a. draw, at the
Phcenix Athletic Club to-night. The fight,
it was announced, was for. the 122-pound
championship of the. world. In the second
round Ritchey had considerably the bet
ter of the in-fighting, but the round ended
with honors even. The third round ended
.slightly in favor of Ritchey, but the gorg
found him on the ropes' from a left to the
jaw. In the fifth Yanger rushed himself
to the floor and Ritchey planted a strong
left to his jaw when he came up. In the
eighth Ritchey floored Yanger with a ter
rible left jolt to the jaw, but the Chica
goan was up immediately, and rushed
Kitcney, who fell to his knees. Both were
on the ropes when the gong- rang-
Ritchey opened the twelfth with a rush,
but v.as fought to the ropes by Yanger.
1 anger put .Ritchey down with straight
lefts to the face and stomach. Ritchey
was slightly groggy and the round was
all in Yanger's favor. Both men slipped
to the floor in rushes during the four
teenth round, but neither was hurt. After
the .sixteenth round both men were bleed
ing. The seventeenth, eighteenth, nine
teenth and twentieth rounds were evenly
contested and the referee aeclared the
fiÂ£ht a draw. t . .

ATIETLETES WILL WRESTLE
BETORE THE FAIR SEX
Olympic Club Tournament Promises
' to Be an Exciting and Inter
esting One.
Â¦ All' is 1n readiness for the wrestling
tournament which is to be held In ' the
gymnasium of the Olympic Club to-mor
row night in honor of the fair sex. The
athletes who' are entered are trained to
the hour and eager for the signal to dis
play their prowess on the mat. John A.
Hammersmith, the leader of the club,
under whose direction the contests will
be held, guarantees that the evenings
entertainment will surpass any ever giv
en for the edification of the ladies.
Credit is due George S. Miehling, wrest
ling instructor of the club, for the splen
did development of the contestants in this
great sport. For the past year he has
labored on the mat with the young ath
letes and taught them all the tricks of
the game. The members who are to take
part in the tournament have attained
high development in wrestling. They are
to wrestle six-minute bouts, and as vic
tory will depend on speed and skill the
spectators will have an opportunity of
seeing some fast and clever work.
George W. Braun is looked upon as a
sure winner in. the 135-pound class. Ex
perts say he is the best wrestler at his
weight ever developed in the Olympic
Club. W. R. McCullough has improved
greatly since the last tournament and is
certain to give the heavyweights a hard
tussle. Never before has there been so
many Heavyweight wrestlers in an Olym
pic Club tournament. There are three
who weigh more than 200 pounds each. H.
M. Plfltt of the I^eliance AtWetic Club and

BY Fl-E. MUXHOIXAND.
NOT so many days, ago Barney
Schreiber purchased the Himyar
mare Jennie for $400, Intending to
install her in the harem at
"Woodlands Farm, Missouri. Pos
sibly thinking she might earn railroad
fare. Trainer Felix Carr dropped her in
-Ihe six furlongr sprint, fourth on the card
&t Tanforan yesterday, for which Her
Â¦Inoso reigned a 9 to 10 chance. Meehanus,
It was figured, might slip in first, but
,against Jennie, even with Dominick in
.the saddle, 30 to 1 went begging. Ever
;Â©n the alert at the barrier, Dominick got
; Kls mount away in the lead, and with
'Hermoso not diseased with speed, showed
the way throughout, winning by half a
length from Doublet, a 20 to 1 shot. Her
moso ran third. Billy Cahill iried a new
*f angled boot on Rinaldo, but the bla-zed
tared horse didn't relish it, endeavoring
To carry both boot and boy over the out
>ide fence, and ruining all chances Mee
Â¦fianus may have had as well.
; This was only one of the mishaps favor
ites met with. lnnocencia, , El Karn,
Malay and Companion fell in for exten
sive backing and all pulled up very dusty
looking without the purse. Nash Turner
was the only rider to pilot two winners.
Â¦Dominick having an off day.
Â¦'Â¦"Naturally -enough El Karn, the mount
of Dominick, was installed favorite for
the five furlong scramble that opened the
, lacing. Uf the gelding had any speed it
was left in the stable. Eidred, ridden by
Conley, led into the stretch, when Nash
Turner, on Bill Young, a warm tip, as
sumed the front position, beating out
Cambaceres a head. The latter was much
the best, coming from far in the rear.
Na?b Turner, on Elmwood Farm's Tiger
<co!t The Giver, beat the favorite lnno
cencia a head for- the two-year-old dash
at four furlongs. The winner carried
much wise support at odds of 4 and 5 to
1, and pot up just in time under hard rid
ing. Murcia, a smart filly from Ab.
Stemler's stable, played on some fast
Â¦workouts, worked her way from, back in
the bunch, finishing third.
Beaten on the Saturday previous, when
backed off the boards, Parader came
Â¦through in the seven furlong selling af
fair;- downing a cheap assortment. The
.big gelding went to the post favorite over
â¢ Midian and at the wire won easily. Alex
ander finished third on Rainier, a 12 to 1
outsider.
- Making his reappearance after a long
Season on the shelf, Gotobed, with "Jess"
Conley up, came home an easy winner of
the mile and a sixteenth run Prejudice
and Malay enjoyed the call, it being 5 to
2 and take your pick. The t*o choices
raced themselves to a standstill, and at
the close Gotobed scored in a big gallop
at odds of 4 to 1. Malay lasted just long
enough to take the place from Dr. Ber
*iays. . . . .
An ordinary set of platers lined up in
Ihe final five furlong scramble. Compan
ion ruled favorite, but quit, and Quibo
beat Aphrodis out in a drive. The win
ner was a 6 to 1 chance.

KANSAS CITY, Mo., April 22.â Mrs.
Carrie Nation's visit- to Kansas City _ two
Sundays ago and her arrest has resulted
in a â¢ strong movement against the Sun
day saloon that may end in bringing to
a definite issue . the fight between the
Kansas joint-smasher and the police. Â«. ,
Yesterday several local ministers
preached strong sermons denouncing the
police for making the arrest and Police
Judge McAuley for fining Mrs- Nation for
no other reason than that she gathered
a crowd about her by denouncing a sa
loon-keeper for exhibiting nude pictures
in his place.
To-day the Ministers' Alliance, at the
regular weekly meeting, drafted a peti
tion to the Mayor and Board of Police
Commissioners, praying that they enforce
the State law against Sunday opening,
and a strenuous effort is to be made by
church people generally to aid the move
ment. Â¦ â¢'
Judge McAuley, when he fined Mrs. Na
tion 1500, threatened Â¦ her with arrest
should she return to the city, and Chief
of Police Hayes emphasized the court's
warning. Notwithstanding this Mrs. Na
tion has agreed to lecture here before the
Union -Mission next Friday night and the
church people are said to have planned
to bring the matter to an issue with the
Police Department by meeting her at the
depot with a brass band and escorting
her about town.
Kansas City Expects to Have
Another Interesting
Time.

CHINESE SLAVES MAY
,; BE ADMITTED TO BAlL
Iheir Photographs Will. Be Taken to
the Secretary of the Navy.
The board of directors of the Manufac
turers' and Producers* Association met
yesterday afternoon for the purpose of
discussing the propriety of making repre
sentations to the Navy Department in re
gard to having ships of the United States
navy stationed in Pacific waters repaire<l
at this port. I By unanimous vote the sec
retary was directed to send the following
letter to Secretary of the Navy Long:-,
The board of directors of this association,
having had under consideration the subject of
future repairs to. ships of the United States
navy, now stationed and hereafter to be em
ployed in the Philippine Islands, in Asiatic
waters and generally in the Pacific Ocean, de
sires to indorse the representations which have
been made to your department by the San
Francisco Chamber of Commerce under date at
April 16 Ia3t, and to most earnestly express
to you the sentiment of this association and of
this State generally that, owing to the nearness
of Pacific Coast ports to the Orient and for the
other reasons stated, it la most appropriate and
also in the Interests of economy that repairs
to such' ships should be made at such ports,
so far as compatible with the requirements of
the service, instead of sending such ships to
Atlantic ports for that purpose.
. The large navy yard at Mare Island, as well
as- the other existing facilities of this port,
enable vessels of all classes to be repaired here
expeditiously and with economy, as has been
demonstrated heretofore, -and It Is the spirit of
our local merchants and manufacturers, if
offered sufficient encouragement, to remain
equipped and to hold themselves In readiness to
supply material, etc., required In, such repairs
on short notice.

The trial of Albert Moody^formerly a
jockey, on a charge of assault to murder,
was commenced before a jury in Judge
Cock's court yesterday morning. Assist
ant District Attorney Deuprey conducted
the prosecution and the defendant was
represented by Senator Porter Ashe.
On the morning of July 4 last, while
Mrs. .Lulu St. D. Roylance was leaving
the. Winchester House, where her hus
band was rooming, she met Moody, who
had apparently been waiting for her. Bhe
was accompanied by Harold S. Adams,
a friend. Moody fired three shots at Mrs.
Roylance, each taking effect. He also
shot â¢ and wounded Adams. The woman
made a statement. In which she declared
that Mocdy had shot her. Tiiey had been
intimate in their relations for some time,
and it was the knowledge that she in
tended to return to her husband that
caused Moody to attempt to kill her.
Since the shooting Mrs. Roylance has
obtained a divorce from her husband,
Leon St. Denis Roylance, * and has en
deavored to stop the District Attorney
and Judge Cook from proceeding with
the case against Moody. While testify
ing yesterday she denied all knowledge
ot the statement made by her and ad
mitted the intimate relations -that had
existed between her and the defendant.

" No gold medal from the Paris Exposi
tion testifying to the excellence of the
statistics' of the local Board of Health
will be hefted around as a part of the
archives of that body. This was decided
upon at a meeting of the board held yes
terday. The Mayor sent in a long com
munication about the proposition to } buy
the one awarded, but by a unanimous vote
it was decided that the medal was too
heavy to carry, with the funds available
under the present appropriation. The
metal testimonial will remain in Paris un
til some one comes forward with sufficient
civic pride to buy It. Â¦'Â¦ .

USES A BEER FAUCET
TO OITSET A EBVOLVEK
Quarrel in a Barbary Coast Resort
Results in the Principals Be
ing Arrested.
Otto Shmidt, a bartender in "Cowboy
Mag's" salo'on on Pacific street, near
Kearny, and George Green, ' a visitor at
the place, were locked up last night on
a charge of assault with a deadly weapon,
Green for threatening Shmidt with a beer
faucet and Shmidt for firing two shots
at Green. The two men got into an alter
cation and Green left the place with the
announcement that he was going after
a pistol. A beer faucet was as near as he
could get to it, and with that he returned
and proceeded to assault Shmidt. Shmidt
drew a pistol of his own and sent two
shots after Green, but neither hit any
thing. Before the row became more seri
ous' the two men were arrested.

Bill Removing Limit on
Height of Buildings
Passed.
Ordinance Providing for Re
turn of Bail Moneys
Adopted.
The bill removing the limitation on the
height of fireproof buildings and limiting
the height of other buildings to 110 feet
was passed to print by the Board of Su
pervisors yesterday. Charles Alpers, on
behalf of the Hayes Valley Improvement
Company, opposed its passage.
The Finance Committee's recommenda
tion that the bill providing for the pay
ment of old claims against the city be laid
over until after the fixing of the next tax
budget was adopted.
An ordinance providing for the with
drawal of moneys deposited as bail with
the warrant and bond clerk of the Police
Court and defining the duties of the Po
lice Judge, Auditor, Treasurer and war
rant and bond clerk was passed to print.
The ordinance provides for the auditing
of the order for bail moneys by the Audi
tor, who Is to be furnished dally with a
complete list of the names of all persons
for whose release ball has been deposited.
The bill providing for the payment of
fees for building alterations under the
eupervision of the Board of Works was
finally passed. McCarthy and Branden
fctein opposed the imposition of fees to
pay the salaries of the building inspectors
on the ground that the expense should
be borne by the general taxpayer. The
bill was defeated on first rollca.ll, but
D'Ascona, who came in late, voted in
favor and made the necessary tenth vote.
Bill Regulating Signs Passed.
The bill submitted by the Merchants'
Association, intended to regulate signs
and transparencies extending over side
walks, was iinally passed.
The acceptance of the right of way for
& culvert in Trocadero Gulch was finally
ordered.
The consideration of the ordinance pro
viding for an increase of wages for jani
tors and elevator men from $2 per day
to ?70 per month was deferred until the
first Monday in May.
The petition of J. W. Goldstein and oth
ers for dismissal and waiver of appeal
taken by the City Attorney in the con
demnation proceedings for St. Mary's
Park was referred to the Judiciary Com
mittee.
The Board of Works was directed to re
quire the owners of the property at 633
Third street to put the sidewalks into
cood condition.
The ordinance providing for the regu
lation and control of the storage of crude
petroleum and explosives under the su
pervision of the Fire Marshal was passed
to print.
The ordinance establishing certain regu
lations concerning the public and private
sewers find drains, and providing a pen
alty for their violation, was passed to
print
The ordinance accepting a grant of a
right of way' at Bernal Station, executed
by the Southern Pacific Company, was
passed to print.
Flood Ghets His Permit.
J. L. Flood -was granted permission to
erect a fireproof building, 180 feet in
height, on the lot at Market and Powell
streets. Similar permission was granted
to C. E. Green and H. T. Scott to erect
a building at the corner of Geary and
Pcwell streets.
The Hayes Valley Athletic Club was
granted permission to hold an amateur
boxing exhibition on April 25. Tobin said
he would vote against allowing amateur
contests in the future, as he considered
they -rrere of a brutal character.
The demand of R. H. Powell for pay
ment to him of J1S for attendance as a
trial juror in criminal cases in the Supe
rior Court was passed with the under
standing that the Auditor shall refuse to
audit the demand in order to test the
validity of the claim.
The City Attorney was directed to ren
der an opinion as to the legality of a
claim of 54380 submitted by the State Lun
acy Commission for the support of crimi
nal insane committed from this citv.
The petition of F. Saunders for the
maintenance of an engine and boiler at
1133 Alabama street, to be used 'in con
nection with a fruit-preserving plant, was
denied. A number of property owners in
the Immediata vicinity opposed the grant
ing of the petition.

LOST HX A FOREST
IN THE CITT LIMITS
Coroner's Deputies Wander About tho
Dismal Woods With a Suicide's
Body.
It sounds like a surprise when the state
ment is made that any adult of sound
mind and well acquainted with the city
should get lost within its limits, yet that
is what happened yesterday morning to
Deputy Coroner Meehan and Morgue Mes
senger Thomas Smith.
They left the i Morgue at midnight of
Sunday to -get the dead body of Frederick
"Weidenhoefer, which had been found in
the gum and cypress forest on the Spring
Valley reserve, three and a half miles
west of the Ingleside Road House and one
mile north of the old Ocean House Road.
They were met at the Ingleside House by
Police Patrolman King, who piloted them
to the spot where the dead body lay. On
coming back through the dense woods the
party lost its way and wandered about in
the darkness for two hours before Police
man King struck the trail that led into
civilization. Meehan and Smith, hungry,
thirsty and half frozen, arrived at the
Morgue with their grewsome load at 7:30
o'clock in the morning, having been seven
hours and a half on the trip, and all of
that period within the city limits.
The body of the dead man was identified
"Wants Match With. McGovern.
Frank Carrils, manager of Herrera, the
Eakersfield featherweight who handily
defeated Toby Irwin a week ago, claims
he is willing to post a certified check for
$1000 with The Call to bind a match be
tween his protege and Terry McGovern.
Carrils also says he will make a side bet
as high as $5000 that Herrera can defeat
the Brooklyn whirlwind. He has dele
gated Judd Gibbs, the local fight pro
moter, to make the match. Gibbs says
that the residents of Bakersfleld look
upon Herrera as a wonder, and are will
ing to wager thousands of dollars on him
whenever he fights.

The mother of a large family of boys,
who takes them all to the barbers Satur
day afternoons to . have their hair cut.
has had that 10-cent raise tacked onto
the price of trimming her darling's locks
and must in the future pay a quarter for
the attention. The barbers contend that
the youngsters' heads are as large as
their papa's and in most cases have a
heavier covering.
The fear engendered in the minds of
those who patronize the barbers that they,
would have to pay a quarter to have their
beards removed was allayed at the meet
of the Barbers' Union last night. The
journeymen decided that/ to wait fifteen
or twenty minutes to hear a whlte-coateu
tonsorlalist cry "next" was worth a lit
tle something and decided that 15 cents
should be the standard price.

known at "White .Hat McCar
thy." had an excitlns adventure
on Geary street early yesterday
morning. He drove his horse
through the windows of the tailor shop
of A. H. Eehm, at 409 Geary street, and
came near Bearing: into fits the women
employed therein. Tailor Behm was han
dling the "goose" when he saw the horse
coming through the window. He sounded
the alarm, and Miss B. Haundrop, who
was sitting in front of the Â¦window, and
her mother, who was a flew feet away,
barely escaped being cut by the flying
glass.
From just what direction McCarthy
came Is not definitely known. Some say
that he came down from Post street along
Mason, and that the horse, smelling a
square meal In the stable at 814 Geary
street, made a dash in that direction. Mc-
Carthy attempted to guide the frightened
animal against a telegraph pole, but
missed the mark and crashed through the
windows of the tailor shop. No one was
injured except the horse, although several
persons had narrow escanes.

\ THE MERGES OF WOMEN J
1 "I am so nervous and wretched." "I feel as if I should I
J fly." How familiar these expressions are! Little things J
annoy you and make you irritable. You can't sleep, you are
unfit for ordinary duties, and are subject to dizziness.
That bearing-down sensation helps to make you feel
.miserable. . .
You have backache and pains low down in the side, pain
I in top of head, later on at the base of the brain.
* Such a condition points unerringly to serious uterine

CHANCE TO SMILE.
Only a short time ago The Call directed attention
to a report. that the great Metropolitan Opdfa-house
in New York, the most sumptuous home of grand
opera in this country, was about to pass into the hands
of a company that would use it for vaudeville enter
tainments at popular prices. That was but one of the
many signs of the time. All over the United States
the same tendency is observable. Commenting upon
the situation in Boston "the Transcript says: "Actor
after, actor, singer after singer, musician after musi-
The change has been shown by the rapidly increas
ing'scope and prestige of the vaudeville in the field
of dramatic art. The time is not very far back in the
psst.when a vaudeville Was hardly recognized as a'
genuine theater. It occupied a vague ground some
where between Shakespeare and a one-ring circus.
It was a stage where clowns, acrobats, trick dogs and
comic singers were combined to give a variety enter
tainment for people who had no other desire than to
laugh. During the last few years a remarkable devel
opment has taken place. The vaudeville has grown
at the expense of the legitimate drama and of opera,
until to-day some of the foremost actors and singers
of the world do not hesitate to accept. vaudeville en
gagements. With the improved character of the per
formances there has come an improved audience. Not
long ago the vaudeville was something that "society"
did not recognize. At present in several of the larger
cities of the country a y au deville audience includes
many of the most fastidious of theater-goers.
WHILE the public has been discussing the "ele
vation of the stage," and suggestions have been
made again and again of the desirability of es
tablishing an endowed theater for the production of
"legitimate drama," a change has been going on in
the* theatrical world which, if not checked or diverted
from its present course, will leave us in a compara
tively few years with hardly any old-school stage to
be elevated or any legitimate drama to be endowed.
THE VAUDEVILLE STAGE.
We have desired to see this issue closed "as a part
of home; politics, since'its reopening at any time in
the future will be costly in many ways. It is of such
supreme importance as to warrant an amendment of
the constitution, if necessary, to quiet forever any
agitation for statehood.
Therefore, our sovereignty in the Philippines is not
interpreted by us to mean there what it means at home,
and among and over ourselves. A careful reading of
the Filipino general's language clearly discloses that
it contains the germs of future trouble, since it leaves
open to him,.or to any others, the way to interpret
denial of statehood, citizenship and the liberties they
promise as a violation of the promises to which he so
vaguely refers. . v
We believe that statehood has at no time been con
templated, either by Congress or the administration.
Indeed, it is not left at all doubtful, for the administra
tion in its answer to the several suits now before the
Supreme Court has distinctly antagonized the theory
that the constitution follows the flag, and has dis
claimed the belief that Porto Rico and the Philippines
are potential states.
It announces a surrender to irresistible force and
the belief that under '.'the sovereign banner of Lhe
United States the Filipino people will attain all those
promised liberties they are beginning toÂ» enjoy." Until
the' American, and. Filipino people have a common
understanding of those promised liberties it is im
possible to predict permanent results and lasting
peace. We may not be mistaken in saying that it is
not trie intention ofthe American people to admit the
islands as states and their people as equal citizens!
The racial and commercial problems which would
generate in such a policy are too grave for it. to be
feasible. If Aguinaldo intend to hold out to his coun
trymen that statehood and full citizenship are near or
remote his manifesto is full of future mischief and
c.oes not deserve to be taken at high value.
IT may well be that the importance of Aguinaldo's
proclamation remains to be demonstrated. Its best
effects may not be its immediate and present influ
ence. It lacks that spontaneity and frankness which
are based only on the ceitain and absolute.
AGUINALDO'S MANIFESTO.
For. the Europeans, living in these colo
nies,- civil, commercial and criminal law
is administered/ as far as is compatible
with the surroundings, in, accordance with
the laws of the, mother country. For the
Double Judicial Systems.
Again the Dutch " have given evidence
of . great tact in Â¦ not following the same
policy in all districts, awaiting their time
and only imposing their authority , when
and where crcumstances have been most
favorable. And it is thus that the great
er part of Java and Madura, in spite of
the dual administration, is under the ab
solute of the Dutch, although
some districts enjoy a certain amount of
independence. In the other possessions,
out- of Java and Madurk, Â¦ the - independ
ence of the native . princes is still far
greater and the dominion Â¦ of the Dutch
but nominal. Â¦
From the time of the English rule the
native princes were styled ""regents"and
the Dutch Governors of the various prov
inces "residents." The princes held their
power by virtue of letters patent from
the sovereign of the Netherlands and be
came in reality . functionaries of the sov
ereign. Though the princes were divested
of their own sovereignty by one fell blow,
the .Dutch were wise enough to respect
scrupulously all their traditions, manners,
laws, customs and usages in all walks of
life. Their titles, marks of. distinction,
sumptuous : manners of living, the ..pomp
and ceremony, and etiquette, of the va
rious courts have always been carefully
preserved and zealously protected, but at
the same time in- each province and by
the side of each regent there is a Dutch
Deputy Governor. This latter function
ary keeps himself quite in the dark and
appears modest and . unassuming, . yet he
is the ruling power and, in order to sat
isfy the native population, he is : known
as the "elder brother" of the prince, for
according to Mohammedan law. . prevalent
in the Dutch East Indies, the elder broth
er has authority over the younger.
Position of Native Princes.
The Governor General, as representative
of the crown, is practically an absolute
monarch. It is auite evident that such
powers, reaching over a population of 35,
000,000, necessitates the creation of a great
corps of functionaries to assist in the ad
ministration. Beyond this centralized
Government, which is- purely Duch and
has its headquarters at Batavia, the capi
tal of the Dutch East Indies, we find
the administration following immediately
on its heels. This is by far the most
interesting feature of the Dutch colonial
system, adopted as far back as the old
days of the Dutch East India Company,
which,' like its successors, gave predomi
nance in all colonial matters to those af
fairs that directly interested the commer
cial and financial side of their enterprise.
The colonial government is divided into
two departments â namely, the home, gov
ernment and the local government; the
latter being subdivided into the European
and native administrations. At home the
supreme colonial power is vested in the
sovereign, at the present moment Queen
Wllhelmlna, but the political responsi
bility of her acts, as iar as colonial mat
ters are concerned, is vested in the Min
ister of the Colonies, the present Minister
being J. T. Cremer, a comparatively
young man and a very able one. All royal
decrees relating to the colonies must be
countersigned by the Minister of the Colo
nies, who is, by. virtue of his responsibil
ity, at liberty to refuse his signature. It
is the Minister, who is responsible to the
States General as well as to the sovereign,
and the latter's administrative and execu
tive power is wholly transferred to the
Governor General of the Dutch East
Indies, to whom all colonial subjects owe
the same respect and obedience as to their
sovereign. This high official is' appointed
by the sovereign upon the recommenda
tion of the cabinet, but the sovereign re
serves the right of calling upon him to re
sign when he thinks such a step neces
sary. .
fact, throughout the Dutch East Indies,
two distinct administrations, the one Eu
ropean with the master hanQ, the other
composed of native princes and based on
the native feudalism. The natives being
thus in contact only with the second ad
ministrative power believe they are sub
ject only to their natural masters, never
realizing for a moment that their own
princes .are but humble subjects of .the
sovereign of the Netherlands. It is, there
fore, in the above dual system that we
'find the true source of the colonial suc
cess of the- Dutch.
It is believed that Sumatra was once the
center of a vast Malay empire, enjoying a
considerable degree of civilization and
practicing the arts and commercial pur
suits. Except for some architectural
ruins, there is now no trace of this an
cient civilization. Although the popula
tion is chiefly composed of Mohammedan
Malays, the inhabitants of Acheen show
a marked difference from those of other
districts. It is these Achinese, who con

tory of 2200 square miles. It has been Dre

The Hollanders have not yet succeeded
in conquering the natives of Sumatra, and
the sovereignty of Tha Netherlands over
that Island is only nominal. Sumatra is
really a vast part of the Dutch East In
dian possessions and thirteen times larger
than Holland. The independent popula
tions in the northern districts have re
fused to cede their right to self-govern
ment. The Netherlands Government, in
the frequent conflicts following the year
1873, has learned this to its cost and as a
consequence the remarkable Dutch sys
tem of colonization has never had a fair
trial throughout the island. The Achinese
still remain defiant, but when once con
quered, it Is to be expected that the Dutch
will apply their favorite schemes here as
they have done with such good results in
Java and Madura.
Conditions in Sumatra.
At the present time the greater part of
the trade of Java and Madura is carried
on directly with Holland, but since the
greater commercial liberty established in
1872 the trade with Great Britain has
taken large proportions. England buys
the raw sugar, which she â¢ exchanges for
cotton goods and hardware, while the
United States, France and China have an
important trade with the East Indian
islands. In 1S99 the customs receipts of
Java and Madura were $4,193,330. From the
sale of coffee, salt, sugar, bark and tin
the Government realized in 1900 nearly $50,
000,000, while the expenditures were about
5600,000 more. In 18SS the exports, in addi
tion to the above mentioned items, includ
ing tobacco, indigo, gums and spices, for
Java and Madura alone amounted to $30,
750,000, while the imports amounted to
about $75,000,000.
All this tropical wealth is protected by
an army of 34,000 men, of whom 18,000 are
Europeans, commanded by a lieutenant
general, who ls,commancler-in-chlef of the
colonial forces i and Minister of "War for
the Colonies, and by a navy of eighteen
war .vessels manned by 2225 meh, all of
whom belong to the mother country. Be
sides these war vessels there is a small
fleet of state corvettes that do police duty
along the coasts.
And now we come to the practical part
of this great colonial work of the hardy
little Dutch nationâ the trade of Java,
the source of the greatest riches that
have been amassed In Holland.
The Commerce of the Indies.
Oriental "isms" nourish without let or
hindrance and Christian missionaries are
forbidden to attempt proselytism, being
only allowed to minister to Christian com
munities already existing.
From what is now occurring in the older States
California ought to learn a profitable lesson. It is
an easy and an agreeable thing to say that the disas
ters are only added proofs of the superiority of our
climate, and to rejoice that we do not have that kind
of incoming of April and May. There is, however,
something more in th- problem than climate. As
yet our hills and mountiins retain their forests and our
streams have not become wholly torrential. The
waste of forests is. however, going on upon the Pacific
Coast with an alarming rapidity. A few more decades
without adequate protection for our woods and the
story of April and May on this coast will not be unlike
that now coming from the banks of the Ohio.
These disastrous floods over the whole reach of the
well settled parts of the United States are not due
wholly to the weather. To a large extent they are
the outcome of man's folly. All through those States,
from Maine to Ohio, men have cut away or burned
or in other ways wasted the forests that once
slocd upon the hillsides and upon the mountains. In
times past the forests served as natural reservoirs of
the water released by melting snows or the falling
spring rains. Now that they have been destroyed the
Maters rush down the channels of the streams in tor
rential wildncss. floods follow and new losses to farm
rnd to factory are added to the direct losses caused
by the waste of the woods.
Now, as we approach the beginning of May, come
reports of even wilder snd wider floods and more
extensive losses from a!l the region extending from
Western Pennsylvania lo Western Ohio. The streams
rre running in roaring torrents down from the moun
tains and the hills and even such large rivers as the
Ohio are not able to carry off the water rapidly
enough to prevent disaster. In fact, the Ohio is at
iSood height. The swollen and still swelling waters
*re pressing against the tops of the levees, and so nenr
rrd imminent is the danger that the passing of steam-'
boats threatens to ad-1 just the final pressure of force
reeded to send the waves, over the banks. The watch
ful residents along the river perceive the danger, and
we learn from the dispatches that. in their desperation
they have several times fired upon passing steamers
as a warning to them not to come near the shore.
AT the beginning of April reports came from
New England of storms of wind and rain ex
tending from Maine to Connecticut. All the
streams and rivers rose to flood height; many bridges
were carried away. In several of the manufacturing
towns the mills were flooded and work stopped for
a number of days. The damage was not great in any
one locality, but in the aggregate it amounted to a
vast sum, and not a fev lives were lost, so that the
arrival of spring in that section of the Union was not
an occasion for festivity.
THE EASTERN SPRINGTIME.
MINERAL ON SCHOOL LANDâSub
scriber, Cal. This correspondent writes:
"A man holds a piece of State school land
on which 20 per cent has been paid and
the deed is recorded. A man, not the one
holding the deed, flnd3 gold-bearing quartz
on the land. Can he take up a claim upon
it?" The rule Is settled that mineral lands
are exempt from the grants of school
lands to the State. Consequently If min
erals should be embraced in any of the
school lands the title does not pass to the
State (Llndley on Mines, 136. A standard
work on mines). In pursuance of this rule
It was held In Mining Company vs. Con
solidated Mining Company, 102 U. S., 167,
where there was a controversy, one claim
ing under a patent of school land from the
State and one claiming the same property
under the mining laws of the United
States, that the title of the State claimant
must yield, as the mineral portion never
vested in the State. This case was fol
lowed In Hermocilla vs. Hubbell, 89 Cal..
5. It would seem from this that a party
can take up a mineral claim under the
laws of the United States on school lands,
even though the land has been sold by the
State.
