The decisions have occasioned a good deal of at
tention in the Eastern ' States, where there are living
a large number of people who have obtained divorces
in States other than those in which they had a matri
monial domicile. The effect will, of course, be to put
a check upon that method of getting divorce, and
accordingly that particular problem may be said to
have been settled. â¢ .
In the third case both the wife and the husband
lived in Kentucky for a time, but the wife left the
home and went to New York to" reside. The husband
sought and obtained a divorce in Kentucky. The
Supreme Court held that to be a valid divorce, be
cause the proceedings were taken in a State where
both, parties had a matrimonial domicile up to the
period of desertion.
According to the reports from Washington the Su
preme Court in deciding the cases declared "that
decrees of divorce granted anywhere except in the
matrimonial domicile oÂ£Â»the parties concerned are not
entitled to faith and credit in the courts of any jurisdic
tion other than that in which the dissolution of the
marriage bond was obtained." By the phrase "matr
rimonial domicile" i? meant a place of residence where
the parties to an action in divorce have lived together
as husband and wife and with the intention to make
the same their home. A domicile acquired in a new
jurisdiction by either husband or wife alone will not
satisfy the conditions required to validate a divorce
outside of that jurisdiction. .
In two of the casesâ cne from New York aifd one
from New Jersey â the question involved the validity,
of divorces granted in Dakota when only one of the
parties to the divorce was a resident of that State.
It appears that in one case the husband and in the
other the wife left home and went to Dakota, where,
after living long' enough to attain a legal residence, a
divorce was obtained. On returning to their former
homes the parties found that the courts of New York
and New Jersey would not recognize ' the Dakota
divorce, and accordingly an appeal was taken to the
Supreme Court of the United States on tlje ground
that the action of the State tribunals was unconstitu
tional, inasmuch as they refused to accept properly
authenticated decrees of the courts of other States
as legal evidence of divorcement. ..
'It now appears thai something may be accomplished
in the way of bringing, order put of chaos by the
Supreme Court of the United States. That tribunal
has recently given decisions in three divorce cases
which not only serve to settle some disputed points
growing out of the varying laws of the different States,
but may prove to be precedents out of which will
develop further decisions so far reaching in their effect
as to virtually provide something' like uniformity of
divorce and marriage in all the States.
THE PROBLEM. OF DIVORCE.
|â J OR a long time p?st there has been an agitation
J for the establishment of something like uniform
laws governing marriage and divorce throughout
the United States. The efforts of the reformers have
been to induce one State after another to enact such
laws in accord with a system agreed upon. From the
very nature of things their efforts have been fruitless
of any result further than that of making known the
evils of the present condition of affairs, and the out
look of the (movement, for reform has never been
bright enough to encourage practical legislators to de
vote much of their time and energy to the cause. .

their own institutions, customs and relig
ious obligations in so far as these har
monize with the acknowledged universal
principles of justice. Discretion is given
to the Judge to decide what religion . or
custom shall arovern each particular case
and when we Take into consideration how
vast are' the eastern possessions of the
little Dutch kingdom, how great are the
varieties of race, of local customs, of re
ligious and social obligations, some Idea
can be formed of the extent of this dis
cretionary power. In 1873 the European
criminal code was applied to all the in
habitants of the Dutch possessions, with
the onlr difference that whereas the Eu
ropeans are punished by imprisonment the
natives are punished by forced labor with
or without chains. The system of tri
bunals and of court precedure of Euro
peans is similar in all its decrees to the
courts of Holland.
In the vital question of education, the
rock-foundation of the future, the Dutch
have put their principles of colonization
severely.to the test. In this, as in all other
matters, their determination not to inter
fere with the traditions of the natives has
been the guiding rule, and so the scnoois
are conducted in the native tongue. "One
of the most tactful measures," says an
authority on the subject, "employed by
the Dutch to appease the natives and ease
the burden of Dutch dominion, which the
Dutch ever seek to conceal, is the use of
the native languages in all their relations
and Intercourse with the natives."
Nearly all colonies founded by the great
nations, both in modern as well as in an
cient times, the conquering nation has
forced its language upon the conquered
people, but the Dutch' have found it far
more practical, far more to their advant
age, to learn the languages of the various
races they govern. As an example of this
it may be mentioned that Queen Wllhelm-
Ina is now studying Javanese with great
assiduity. j

Dutch have refrained from interfering in
any way with the native beliefs. Moham

X.â THE DUTCH EAST INDIES.
- In the annals of successful colonization
the small Dutch nation ranks next to
Great Britain. With a population scarcely
larger than that of the city of London she
has planted her standard in a vast region
sixty-five times greater than that of her
own little land of dikes and windmills and
has maintained her sovereignty over 35,
000,000 people en the other side of the
globe. Her tatik has been so. great, so
fraught with difficulties that Dutch colo
nization has become a veritable science.
These colonies have always been admin
istered as conquered possessions, their
government has been autocratic and the
inhabitants have never had a voice In the
form or manner of government to which
they are subjected. Such is the real rela
tionship between theDutch colonizers and
those over whom they rule, 'but the secret
of this policy, so cunning on the part of
the Dutch, consists in covering, over this
unpleasant truth so successfully that the
natives believe themselves subject only to
their natural chiefs, to their own princes
and their national feudalism. In brief, the
Dutch ruler hides the mailed fist beneath
a silken glove by abstaining from all at
tempt at presolytism, moral or religious,
and by paying a scrupulous respect to the
customs and rites of the natives. The sys
tem of rule is really one of absolute sub
jection, in which the power at home im
poses upon the colonies, without Consult
ing them, whatever legislation it thinks
the most in its own interest, while the
colonies themselves are represented neith
er in Parliament nor in the Government
councils.
A Dual System of Government.
Contrary to the French, who have al
ways endeavored to force upon the na
tives whom they govern French manners
and customs and- to make their presence
and their authority felt, the Dutch have
made their presence as little perceptible
and unoffending as possible by establish
ing a dual administration. There are, in

Sixty-Five Times Larger Than
the Home Country.

PAPERS ON CURRENT TOPICS.
In estimating the economy of a municipal water
supply, the expense of which has to be borne by all
the taxpayers, it is not habitual with the advocates of
such policy to consider all the items, or to remember
that the entire cost must be divided between all tax
payers and added to the rate paid for the water which
each consumes and pays for. A municipal plant pays
no taxes, therefore the share which it should bear has
to be borne by other property, and is added to the
ratepayer's burdens. To put a municipal plant in
condition for comparison with one in private or cor
porate ownership, it must be charged with the taxes
which the private plant would pay, and with interest
on' the cost of the plant. It must also be made equal
to the private plant by using the same filtering and
other devices to make the water safe and potable.
AsvAve have shown in the case of Chicago, Washing
ton, Philadelphia, New York and Boston the quality
of the supply is bad and is not bettered by the methods
which public authority compels the private plants to
use. If those methods are adopted in a public supply
their cost and the interest thereori must be paid by
the people, either in an increased price for the water
or in taxation. When all these elements are taken into
the calculation a comparison of cost between the two
methods of supply can be made and not before.
It is probable that an expert inquiry made into the
water administration of Chicago, Philadelphia and
Boston .will expose a similar deficiency .in, revenues^,
and a like offset of .cost to the taxpayer, to balance
apparent economy to the. same taxpayer. as a water
consumer. .
What Mill called "the coarse and careless hand of
government," forced into the transaction of such es
sentially business affairs as public service industries,
is too apt to present a sheet with the balance on the
wrong side.
When this report was submitted-the Merchants' As
sociation passed the following resolution of indorse
ment: "Resolved, That the report showing discrepan
cies in the city revenues, dated November 21, 1900,
â¢^ade *o this board by Mr/ de Berard by direction of
President King, be accepted and at once made public."
In his conclusion the expert, says: "I have cited the
facts as set" forth as evidence that the present system
of public records and reports in use in this city is
insufficient, because it does not record or report the
details of this city's business in such form as to pro
tect the taxpayers against waste and corruption. The
admission of the Commissioner of Water Supply is that
the system under which the business of his department
is carried on does not inform him what the proper
water revenues of the city should be, and that he can
not, therefore, know whether the meter service of
Manhattan should yield $2,500,000 or $5,000,000 per
year. It is obvious that the task of fully collecting the
city's revenue is a difficult one when the official
charged with that duty does not know how much is
due and who owes it."
Taking the metered water as the basis of his investi
gation the expert made an exhaustive inquiry. He
found the water administration and the books and
records in a hopeless condition of confusion, out of
which it was scarcely possible to ascertain the loss to
the taxpayers from a , careless system of collection.
He found that the revenue from metered water should
have been $11,246,162, but that the collections made
and arrearages reported by the Water Registrar
amounted to only $7,10.3.372, leaving a loss to the tax
payers of $4,142,700. t
He found that since 1891 no report of the metered
water and collections has been made at all, and that
the city loses one-third of the amount which it should
collect for such water.
Supervisor Reed, in his interesting communication
on that subject, quotes the statistics of the Merchants'
Association of New York, and shows that municipal
water is cheaper per family than corporate water sup
ply by an average of two and a half cents per day per
family. As the argument of economy to the consumer
is justly potent it becomes necessary to examine the
methods of administration and collection. A munici
pal supply is at the cost of all the taxpayers, as a cor
porate supply is at the cost of all the stockholders of
the corporation. If the corporation is lax in its ad
ministration and its collections the amount apparently
saved by the ratepayers is a loss to the stockholders.
Also, if municipal ownership is careless in administra
tion and collection from the consumers, the loss falls
upon the taxpayers, and in their case reduces the ap
parent saving of two and a half cents per day per
family. If the taxpaying head of a family save two
and a half cents per day on his water bill, and pay out
that amount or any part of it in taxes necessary to pay
the cost of the water supply," the loss in taxes becomes
a part of his water bill. The same Merchants' Asso
ciation quoted by Supervisor Reed instituted an expert
inquiry into the loss to the taxpayers of New York
due to defective administration of the municipal water
supply.
WHEN discussing such an interesting question
as the municipal ownership of public service in
dustries it is necessary to argue from the full
data at hand.

PERSONAL MENTION.
It is too early to undertake to forecast what will
he the result of the new development, or the effect
which' the change will have upon our acting and our
music. It is evident, however, that the United States
is building up a new form of. theatrical entertainment.
It will doubtless continue to bear the old' name of
vaudeville,' but it will_ be something widely different
from what that name signifies in Europe. It is going
to include the highest acting and the best of music,
for American audiences will demand the best; and in
the end the development may prove to be something
the nation may be proud to point to as its achievement
in the line of theatrical art/
cian, has renounced his conventional dignity as a
'legitimate' performer, and has consented to serve the
whims and caprices of vaudeville patrons. Ingoing
so the artists have all btiilded' better than they knew,
for the circle of variety theater-goers has been grad
ually widened under their influence^ until it practically
includes all to whom the footlights make their ap
peal." .

NEWS OF THE OCEAN.
MANY vessels of the coasting fleet
are making long passages and
it is feared that some of them
may have run short of provi
sions if nothing worse has nap
pened. One vessel is almost given up for
lost and the owners would be glad to hear
from a number of others.
The old barkentine Monitor is now out
thirty days from Qrays Harbor for San
Francisco and her owners have almost
given up hope of ever seeing her again.
Before leaving San Francisco on her last
voyage the Monitor was put on the ways
and overhauled. At that time an expert
on hulls said to Captain Donnelly, "If you
should meet with any heavy weather for
God's sake handle her easy." "That's all
right," answered the master, "when it
comes on to blow I'll put her to sleep."
The Monitor and schooner Lettitia left
Grays Harbor In company on March 24
last. The Lettitia made the run down the
coast in six days and has been overhauled
and is ready to start back again, while
the Monitor has not been heard irom
since the Lettitia parted company with
her on March 26. Captain Donnelly was
accompanied by his . wife on this la^t
voyage. '
Of the schooner fleet the Bella- is out
twenty days from San Francisco JuJr Sius
law the Mayflower twenty days for Co
quille, the Lizzie Prien is twenty-four
days out for Siuslaw and the Park
ersburg twenty-four days for Coquille,
while the Nettie Sundborg is sixteen days
and the Wing and Wing twenty-one days
out for Siuslaw. As the Coquille River
is only 360 miles and Siuslaw about 500
miles from here the entire fleet should
have been at its destination long ago.
Both places, however, are very hard to
enter. The bars are bad and if there }s
any heavy weather a vessel has to lie
off and wait for a chance to get in. When
they do get in they very frequently have
to wait for weeks for a chance to get out
again. If they have plenty of provisions
the chances are that the fleet is all right.
CITY OF FUEBI^a. IN COMMISSION
Innovated Puget Sound Liner Now
the Pride of the Fleet.
The Pacific Coast Steamship Com
pany's City of Puebla arrived from Puget
Sound ports yesterday on her maiden trip
since she was overhauled. The Puebla
was sent to Seattle to be repaired. New
boilers were put in, the machinery was
overhauled, bilge-keels to steady the ves
sel were put on and other work done at a
cost of over $100,000. On her trial on the
Sound the Puebla made over eighteen
knots an hour. On the trip down she aver
aged fifteen and a half knots and Captain
Jepeen is of the opinion that she can
easily make sixteen knots an hour on a
continuous run.
Th* State ot California, which took the
Puebla's place on the Sound run, will be
put on the Seattle-Skaguay route so that
she will not be seen in San Francisco
again for some time to come.
News From the Overdues.
It now appears that there was good rea
son for the reinsurance on the British
ship Ardnamurchan. When the news came
that salmon which was supposed to have
been on the vessel had gone ashore on the
Washington coast the underwriter" at
once began covering their risks. The rate
on the vessel went up and down the scale
and when she finally reached port 50 per
cent was the prevailing figure. A cable
received yesterday by the Merchants' Ex
change says that on December 17 last, oft
the Washington coast the Ardnamurchan
was on her beam ends for several hours
and that 5000 cases of salmon had to be
jettisoned in order to save the vessel.
When the news came that the vessel was
safe it was generally supposed that the
salmon washed ashore was not from the
Ardnamurchan. The cable shows that *:he
first impression was the correct one.
The French bark Grande Duchesse Olga,
now out 109 days from Tahiti for EuroDe
and on which 30 per cent reinsurance has
been paid, was spoken on April 11 in lati
tude 39 degrees 12 minutes north, longi
tude 34 degrees 52 minutes west. The Olga
left here with a cargo of wheat and put
into Tahiti dismasted. New spars were
sent out from France and another start
was made.
On the John McDonald, now out 217
days from Baltimore for San Francisco,
85 per cent is being paid and on the Man
chester, 243 days out from New York for
Yokohama, 60 per cent is the ruling
figure.
Water Front Notes.
The steamer George F. Haller with the
cannery tender Mohawk in tow, sailed for
Bristol Bay Sunday, but both were com
pelled to put back to port on account of
the Mohawk springing a leak.
The majority of the sugar fleet has ar
rived after long passages. The best run
for this month is that of the Roderick
Dhu, sixteen days from Hilo. She was
followed in yesterday by the barkentine
Addenda, twenty-four days from Kihei.
and the schooner S. T. Alexander, twenty
three days from Kahului.
The Pacific Mail Company will lay the
Cotan up for repairs and the San Juan
wifflake her place on the Panama run.
Tne Oceanic Steamship Company's Aus
tralia will sail for Tahiti Thursday, April
25. at 2 p. m.
The transport Grant will leave for
Manila next Thursday and will be fol
lowed by the Warren on May 1.

NOTEâ In the above ' exposition of the tfdes
the early morning tides are given in the left

minus (â ) siffn precedes the height, and then
the number given is subtracted from the depth

LIVES AND WAYS
OF WILD ONES
Ernest Seton Thompson
Talks of Animals He
Has Known.
Tells of Old Beasts Made
Famous and Beloved
by His i Books. '
Ernest Seton-Thompson, whose storie3
of wild animals have charmed old and
young, was heard for the first time on
the lecture platform in this city at Met
ropolitan Temple yesterday afternoon.
Delays and complications kept the audi
ence waiting for more than half an hour,
but no one grumbled and all earned thi
compliment "William Greer Harrison paid
to their â¢ patience. Mr. Harrison intro
duced Mr. Seton, for Seton is his name
and Thompson his nom de plume, in a
neat speech," which he strung out* until
the stereopticon had been put into-work
ing order: then the lecturer came for
ward. '
.Mr. Seton-Thompson is a man of- young
appearance with keen observing eyes that
miss nothing and a voice with something
of an accent .'in it. He does not always
speak fluently, but he chooses his words
well and his descriptions of the lives and
habits of 'his friends, the wild 'animals,
are terse and forceful and at times elo
quent. In opening his lecture yesterday
afternoon he said: . '
"1 will speak to-day of the personal side
of one or two of my wild animal friends,
and by personal side I mean their hu
man side. , It is believed by many that
wild animals are nothing more than ma
chines, but we know better. We know
that they think, that they have a mind.
And not only have they a mind and a con
sciousness but a conscience as well, and
they have a sense of humor and of life
and happiness. A dog's idea of happiness
may not be very high; it may not be any
higher than a desire to keep full of beef
steak, but such as it is, it is definite.. That
is what I mean by the personal side Of
wild animals. It is their mind that I am
after."
Characteristics of Fox Tracks.
The lecturer then took up the trail of
a fox, showing pictures of the tracks in
the snow and telling how the actions of
the fox could be guessed by the charac
teristics of the track. Once he stopped
and dug out a garter snake, but he did
not eat it, and farther on he caught a
prairie chicken lying in fancied security
in a drift. All this was shown by -the
tracks of the fox and by the holes in the
snow where the luckless snake and the
prairie chicken were disturbed.
Then he told of the Springfield fox,
which is the subject of one of the stories
in his book, and then he turned to his ex
periences with bears in the Yellowstone
National Park.
In telling of the bears he made frequent
mention of "Little Johnny," the cub or
"Old Grumpy," two well-known charac
ters among the park bears. Among them,
tco, was an immense grizzly that he de
scribed. It was this grizzly that he Aft
erward made famous as "Wab" in one of
his best stories.
He reserved for the last "Lobo," the
gaunt gray old king wolf of New Mex
ico. "Lobo" is told of in his book, but he
amplified the story and made of the
"grand old reprobate" the wild hero he
really was. It was a graphic story, well
and feelingly told, and when the keen,
cruel head of the old cattle thief was
shown upon the screen the audience for
got all but the noble side of the rugged
ranger and applauded him right heartily.
Woes of Some Animals.
Last evening-Mr. Seton-Thompson lec
tured i again, taking as his subject the
name he gave his book, "Wild Animals
I Have Known." He did not adhere to
the text of the bpok, however, although
several of the characters that make those
pages so charming lent their Interest to
his talk last evening. He told again the
woes of little Mollie Cottontail, of Wully
and Bingo, the dogs; of the pacing mus
tang , and of the fox, and to these he
added his experience with antelopes and
deer, a stag hunt and a moose hunt, the
antics of a pet skunk, the voice and ac
tions of a dog on different trails and the
notes of the , birds along the Red River
of Manitoba.
In describing his dog trailing different
animals he wove In the habits of the
coon, the fox, the skunk and the house
hold cat, and he imitated the cries of the
dog as he pursued each of these animals
and how it was that he could tell by the
cries just what the, dog was doing.
Describes a Deer Hunt.
In describing the deer hunt and the
moose mint he imitated the calls of the
animals, their love cries and their chal
lenges, and when he came to the birds
he whistled- and chirped and called like
everything from a chickadee up to the
great white crane. He imitated the
whirr of wings, the calls of the duck, the
song of. the meadowlark and half a dozen
others and the raucous "honk" of the
gray goose flying northward. He con
cluded by describing the killing of a bull
moose after luring him to h}s doom by
imitating his challenges to battle and fin
ally when they failed, by giving the call
of the lovesick cow moose wailing for her
"It took nature ten years to create that
magnificent animal, which hurt no one,"
he said in conclusion, "and in ten minutes
it was laid low and became nothing more
than a heap of butcher's meat."
Mr. Seton-Thompson will lecture again
Saturday afternoon on "Wild Animals ir.
Captivity," " and again Saturday evening
on "Minds of Animals." His afternoon
lecture will be more for the entertain
ment of 'the children, but his evening talk
will be more advanced. The lectures are
being given under the auspices of the
California Club.

Mrs. Madeline Macondray filed suit yes
trrday for divorce from Robert Arthur
Macondray. As cause of action Mrs. Ma
condray alleces cruelty and In her com
plaint cites many specific acts upon the
part of her husband which, she says,
caused her great distress of body and
mind.
By way of introduction to her complaint
Mrs. Macondray states? that she married
her husband August 2S. 1300. Very soon
after their marriage, she continues, the
defendant commenced to treat her cruelly
and continued such conduct up to the
time she was compelled to seek the pro
tection of her mother, who resides at 103
Frederick street.
Mrs. Macondray's charges of cruelty as
pet forth in the complaint are crisp and
brief. She alleges that her husband rep
resented that he was a man of considera
ble means, which he was not: that he
said he had a position in' the Philippines
which would bring him a large income,
which he did run have, and when she ac
tompnnied him to Manila in search of this
phantom she says she was compelled to
leave the islands and start for the United
Rates under humiliating circumstances
and conditions which were extremely mor
tifying to her pride and injurious to her
health. Continuing, Mrs. Macondray says
lipr husband, though "not an habitual
drunkard." is habitually more or less un
der the influence of liquor. When in this
condition he frequently left her for days
at a time, she says, and on returning
would curse and abuse her. much to her
mental distress. When offered employ
inert "by which he could earn an honest
living." Mrs. Macondray says her hus
band refused to accept it, but continued
f n his round of abuse and dissipation un
til she was compelled to seek the pro
tection of her mother. Mrs. W. G. Rich
si rdson. Mrs. Macondray. in conclusion,
prays for a decree of absolute divorce,
ihf right to resume her maiden name and
r.ny further relief which the court may
?cp fit to award her. She waives the right
to an alimony judgment, however, saying
Â¦that her husband has nothing: and hence
a judgment would avail her not.

ray by Susan L. Griffin against Joseph
Criflln for desertion. Annie E. Alexander
nsraSnst O. L. Alexander for cruelty,
I'rankie Stewart against M. C. Stewart
for depcrtion, Walter Cheetham against
Clara Cheotham for desertion. Marie A.

BARKENTINE MONITOR. BOUND FOR SAN FRANCISCO. AND SIX SCHOOONERS BOUND BOR SIUSLAW AND
COQUILLE RIVERS ARE NOW CONSIDERABLY OVERDUE. GRAVE FEARS ARE FELt/fOR THE SAFETY
OF THE MONITOR AND HER CAPTAIN AND CREW.
The, Twenty-sixth regiment is quartered
in upper Model Camp and is thoroughly
protected from invasion by civilians.
Lieutenant Colonel Dickman is another
officer whose conduct meets with the dis
approval of his men and the remarks
made by the soldiers are not at all com
plimentary to the doughty commanding
officer. , â¢
A peculiarity connected with the return
of the volunteers, who have been arriving
from the Philippines of late, has been the
absence of any complaint against the offi
cers of the regiments. This evidence of
the pleasant relations between j officers
and privates, however, does hot apply to
the Twenty-sixth Infantry, which arrived
here on the Garonne. The soldiers or
this Â¦ regiment discuss the actions of the
officers under whom they have served
without reservation and the charges made
are enough to convince a civilian that
life in the army is not a bit pleasant.
"Poor'food and poor treatment is due to
poor officers," say the men, and every
one who has a complaint to make 13 will
ing to make it as soon as the fear of im
mediate punishment for his ' presumption
is removed â that is after he -receives his
discharge.
The rumored prospective court-martial
of Lieutenant Edwards, who, it is alleged,
left the regiment at Nagasaki rather than
face a board composed of his superior
officers, is freely discussed by the men.
They are not a bit backward in referring
to him as a "stomach robber," which in
the parlance of the soldier, means "an
officer who underfeeds- his men."
Privates Claim Commanders
.-, Are Not Fit for Posi
tions. .
Officers of Twenty-Sixth
Infantry Berated by
Men.
SOLDIERS MAKE
MANY CHARGES
The doors of E. O. McCormlck's private
offices were closed and locked yesterday to
the cutside public. The passenger traffic
manager of the Southern Pacific Com
pany, with a few delegates left over from
tlie Transcontinental Passenger Associa
tion meeting that was held in Del Monte
last week, were going over the ground
preparatory to putting in a bid for the
transportation of the naval recruits that
are to be brought out from the East in
July.
At the recent meeting- down in Monterey
last week it was decided by all the roads
that in the future there rhould be no rate
cutting on Government work, or in plain
BnrHrti. that they would stand together
and divide the business. Yesterday the
Santa Fe, Rock Island and Union Pacific,
and in fact nearly all the roads that ope
rate west of the Mississippi River were
represented at the meeting and the busi
ness was sjiMt up.
President Charles M. Hays is at pres
ent in Southern California. He will ar
rive in thi? city to-morrow and his advent
is nmully awaited by quite a few or the
employes of the Southern Pacific. An old
attache of the road said yesterday that
ho had learned that when Hays arrives
ho will make several changes. He will
a!r?o at the same time thoroughly outline
the policy of the road in the future.
Whether 'this information will be given
by an official circular or whether there
will be a tmnquft. at which those who are
to be rewarded or discharged will be let
down easily, remains to be proved.
Manager "Kruttsohnitt is down the road
and will Journey to this city with Hays.
It is said that when he heard of the train
wrecks that are coming fast and thick
on the coast division he packed his dress
f=uit case. and. eailins his stenographer.
Journeyed south to find out where the
trouble lay. Eastern railroad men claim
that the cause of the recent accidents is
the locomotives that are at present on
the road. They say the engines are de
fective.

CHICAGO. April 22.-In the corn pit tho
Phillips deal in May corn came to the fore
after a fortnight, during which lt -.-merely
kept the market nervous, but comparatively
narrow around "44c for May. Up to to-day
the opinion that the bull leader's vast line
had been gradually diminishing under cover
of , brokers had been spreading. This was
merely conjecture, however, for at times,
when . the market seemed inclined to boom'
there were indications that the wily manipu
lator was working to check any sudden
bulge. His announcement that he would take
in every bushel offered him on May con
tracts brought the paramount . importance of
Phillips' continued bullish attitude forcibly
Before traders. May 1, It Is estimated, there
will be 3,000,000 bushels of contract - corn
which can be delivered to him, but even this
would â¢ not satisfy his claims, as shorts have
guaranteed to deliver him between 5 000 000
and 7,000,000 bushels additional. That shorts
will be able' to get that amount here for de
livery before the last of May Is, of course,
food for doubt. The . fact .. that country of
ferings last night were practically nothing
and the receipts to-day were but
cars, with - only eight cars grading contract,
had much to do in alarming the gentlemen
who have on their, shoulders the burden of
getting this corn from country cribs or set
tling with Phillips. They scrambled in wild
confusion to-day for the privilege of taking
the loss already theirs, rather, than stand by
their guns in the hope that he. and his follow
ing would find themselves in deep .water be
fore May 1. To make his position more se
cure, it Is said, Phillips already has con
siderable corn ready for shipment East It
was a noteworthy fact to-day that :. while the
price level nearly the whole session was
higher than at any previous stage of the
crop very little was sold . far from the top
price. Phillips started the excitement by buy
ing at the opening. May opened %<S%c higher
at 44?Â»(Â§>44%c and bounded up to 46%c during
the forenoon. At this price every hull | pit
had a good profit in sight. Their sales caused
a reaction to 46c, but shorts absorbed everv
thinp with Â¦ such avidity that the market i re-'
acted sharply and closed 2c higher at 46%c
July sold between 4i Tic and 43c and closed 'l'ic
hleher at 44%c. . ..,-;,$
Trade in wheat was active. The market
for lt was the broadest in weeks, the activity
being sustained with scarcely â¢ an Interrup
tion. For the - first time In . days the favor
able weather and liberality of receipts were
Â¦ treated with contempt. A sharp advance at
Liverpool, bullish world's statistics and the
prospect of a fair visible decrease were Influ
ential at the start. May opening ',^c to %c
htsrher at 71Vic to "l'ic. . The- neneral demand
was from outsiders and from local shorts as
well as confident longs. During the forenoon
May climbed to 72%c, additional incentive
having been found in reports of a strong con
tinental demand. In the corn strength Rnd In
the visible figures which showed a greater
falling off than had been looked for. . - Realiz
ing sales on a liberal scale caused a reaction
to 72#c nÂ»ar the end of the session, but the
market rallied firmly and closed 144c higher at
72Vic - July closed at 72"ic, compared with

DRIEO FRUITS.

whole was a slow one. Orders for prime goods

other grades were moving slowly In a jobbing

.New York Metal Market.
NEW YORK, April 22.â Quietness was prev
alent In all quarters of the metal trade to
day, owing to a lack of speculative interest.
London advices were without . particular sig
nificance, reporting unchanged prices for tin
at Â£116 7s 6d fcr spot and Â£114 5s for futures.
Locally the market was very quiet, but a shade
easier on bid futures, with final prices at
$'5 90@26 10. Buyers and sellers 'alike were
scarce. Copper was unchanged, but steady in
tone, in sympathy with a rise of 7s 6d In Lon
don. The close here was on the basis of $17
for Lake Superior and 16%c for casting and
electrolytic. Lead remains unchanged Â¦ here,
while prices abroad were five shillings higher
at the close, with spot at 12s 5d. Under a
sheer lack of buying orders prices eased off.
about 5 points in spelter, but the market was
very dull with -the close finally, quiet at $3 95
Â®4 At London an advance of 2s 6d was
scored to Â£17 2s 6d. Conditions prevalent in
foundry iron were much the same as hereto
fore mentioned. Demand was light and prices
held their own. Plglron warrants, $3 oO@10 oO;
Northern foundry, $15 25@16 50; Southern foun
dry, $14@15 50, and soft Southern, $13@lo oO.

New York Cotton Market.
NEW YORK, April 22.â The cotton market
started quiet and firm., with prices 3 points
lower to 4 points higher. A drop of 6@7 points
was of short duration, as covering set in in
the afternoon and continued practically to the
close. On this support prices once more ral
lied to the top. with the market showing a
strong undertone. Taken as a whole, business
was more active than the average of late.
Closed steady, 10@ll points higher.

London Market.
NEW YORK, April 21â The Commercial
Advertiser's London financial cablegram says:
Only a fair business was done on the Stock
Exchange here to-day, and that was irregu
lar In tone. Interest was centered in the is
sue of the Â£60,000,000 of new console at SIM.
half of which was placed before the announce
ment was made. The syndicate to float the
issue is made up of big men in London, on the
Continent and In America. jThe mosf promi
nent names are the Rothschilds, Morgans, Cas
sel, Wernher and Belt, and It is said that. the
whole issue has already been over-subscribed.
It is believed also, however," that the sub
scribers will be disappointed, since so many
the holders of the old consols have sold out
in anticipation of the new issue that the price
of consols has been depressed to 94%. In con
sequence of this the premium of the new issue,
which at the opening was 1 per cent, declined
to one-half per cent.
American shares were subjected Â¦ to several
spasms to-day, but were on the whole fairly
firm until the opening in New York. There
was a sharp break, which was accompanied
by some talk of the floods, which affected
particularly the Atchisons, Eries and Norfolk
and Westerns. After that there was a gen
eral partial rally in the street under the lead
ership of a spurt tn St. Paul. The strongest
feature was Southern Pacific, which was
bought on a tip from' New York. Money is
scarce on a demand for deposit against the
new issue of consols.
Silver was strong on purchases for Â¦ the

3.57MEXICAN BAUT>IT SLAYS
AMERICAN BROTHERS
PHOENIX, A. T.. April 22.â News has
just been received here of the murder of
Frank and Charles Leal by Francisco
Valdez, a Mexican bandit, at Hardshell
mining camp, near Nogales, a few days
ago. The Leals 'were Americans who were
employed at the Hardshell mine. Valdez
started a row with them in a saloon and
then shot them both. After they had
fallen he shot each again through the
head, and then battered their skulls -with
the butt of his revolver.

Taken From Her by Her
Husband, Who Then > 1^
Disappears.
With tears streaming down, her, cheeks
a handsome young woman entered the
office of the Bond and Warrant Clerk in
the Hall of Justice yesterday and asked
for a warrant for the arrest of her hus
band for kidnaping. The woman gave her
name as Maud Shaughnessy and her resi
dence Sacramento. Her husband is Henry
Shaughnessy, who Is well known In the
capital city.'
According to the woman's story she and
her husband and their two-year-old girl
arrived here three days ago from Sacra,:
mento and went to live in a lodging-house
on Eleventh street, near Mission. Short
ly after noon yesterday Shaughnessy In
duced his /wife and child to take a walk
with him. claiming that he was anxious
to see a friend who owed him money. As
they walked down Mission street Shaugh
nessy pretended that he was in need of
a drink of whisky, and picking up his lit
tle daughter he told his wife to wait a
few. minutes until he returned* from, a
saloon close* bv. --
Mrs. Shaughnessy waited for nearly
half an hour for the return of her hus
band and child and then, suspecting some
thing was ' wrong, she went into the
saicon and inquired for her husband. To
her great surprise she learned that he
had left the place by a rear door, taking
the child with him.
Frantic with grief and ! convinced that
she had been deserted and robbed of her
child, Mrs. Shaughnessy hurried to police
headquarters and asked for a warrant
for her husband's arrest.
i After the warrant had been issued Mrs.
Shauerhnessy. while riding in a Powell
stre<it car, espied her husband and child
sitting on the dummy. . The distracted
woman attempted to get possession of her
little girl, calling upon the passengers to
assist her. The conductor, however, took
the part of Shaughnessy and forced the
weman off the car. She managed to se
cure the shawl that was wrapped around
her child, and with this as a souvenir of
tho struggle, went to police headquarters
and pleaded with the officer In charge to
tind her child. â¢ â¢
"I know he intends to keep her from
me." she tearfully cried. "For God's sake
don't let him rob me of my only treas
ure." "Â¦â â¢ Â¦
Captain Seymour at once detailed two
of the men to find Shaughnessy with, in
structions to arrest him and the child.
As it is believed that he intends to re
turn to Sacramento Captain Seymour has
wired to Chief of Police Sullivan of that
city to take him into custody.

MOTHER PLEADS
FOR HER CHILD
WASHINGTON. April 22.â In the) United
States Supreme Court to-day Chief Jus
tice Fuller announced the final adjourn
ment of the court of the term on May 27.
The call of the docket will be suspended
next Friday and the court will take a re
cess next Monday until May 13. No Inti
mation has been given by tbÂ« court as to
when the decision of the insular cases
may be expected, or whether they will be
decided at all before final adjournment.
An opinion was handed down to-day In
the case of H. I. Bedford vs. the Eastern
Building and Loan Association of Syra
cuse, N. Y. This grew out of tho
sale of stock in the building association to
Bedford and the making of a loan to him
in 1S91, he being a resident of Tennessee.
Bedford made default in the payment of
his note and pleaded the violation of a law
of Tennessee requiring the deposit of
funds by building associations doing busi
ness In the State. The court held, how
ever, that the loan was a contract, which
the act of the State Legislature could not
Impair.
The attention of the court was given to
day largely to the question of the right
of one State to quarantine against an
other. The contention arose in connection
with the case K W. P. Smith vs. the St.
Louis and Western Railway Company, in
volving quarantine regulations against
the importation of cattle in 1897. The cat
tle shipment upon which this case turned
was made by Smith over the St. Louis
road from Plain Dealing, La,, to Fort
Worth. Tex., the officials of the road be
ing unaware that a quarantine had been
established, which had been done by the
Texas officials. When the existence of
this restriction became known, the rail
road company refused to deliver the stock
to the consignee and reshlpped them to
Plain Dealing. Smith refused to receive
them, as he also did the proceeds of their
sale. Smith then brought suit for dam
ages, contending that the regulation was
an interference with interstate commerce
and unjustified by the facts.
The trial court sustained this regulation.
but the StatÂ© Court of Appeals reversed
the decision, holding: in favor of the com
pany. To-day's opinion affirmed the lat
ter decision on the ground that the State
has a right, under Its police power, to pro
tect itself against infectious diseases, even
though commerce may be incidentally In
terfered with in the regulations for such
protection. Justices Harlan and White
united In a dissenting opinion and Justice)
Brown delivered a dissenting opinion of
his own.
The principal op'ilon in the case was de
livered by Justice McKenna.
Opinion Is Rendered in' the
Case of Cattle Importa
tion.
Supreme Court Says the
States Can Protect
Themselves..

SMITH IS DEFEATED
AND FATALLY INJURED
New York Pugilist Fares Badly at
the Hands of Jack
Roberts.,
LONDON, April 22.â At the National
Sporting Club, London, to-night, â¢ Jack
Koberts of Eng$land knocked out Billy
Smith, the American, in the eighth round.
The fight was for the nine-stone cham
pionship of England.
During the first three rounds everything
was In favor of Smith, who showed him
self decidedly the more clever of the two
men, but for the remainder of the contest
Roberts had his own way and he dealt
out a lot of severe body punishes. In the
eighth round Smith made no attempt
whatever to defend himself. He was
twice.knocked down and then counted out.
Subsequently it became known that
Smith's right arm was dislocated In the
fourth round, and- at the finish his legs
were badly cramped.
After the fight Smith was removed to
a hospital, where he was still unconscious
at 5 o'clock â¢ this morning. It is feared
that his injuries may prove fatal.
Billy Smith, who has received such seri
ous injuries In his contest with Jack Rob
erts, the undefeated featherweight of
England, is a New Yorker, and resides on
the east side of Manhattan Island. His
fighting in this country before he crossed
tho Atlantic showed him to be a boxer
of promise, and his American " friends
counted on his defeating Roberts, 2 al
though it was expected that he would
have a hard fieht on his hands.

DENVER, April 22.â The Denver Pout
mv.Â«: Georgia Hobson, born two o.-iyn ago
in Los aiiboIos, will rtgum in the most
novel and one of the most sensational law
suits ever tried In the courts of Colorado.
Mrs. Hobson has Bent word from Los
Ang-eles that she proposes to contest the
will of George H. Hobson, her late hus
band, which bequeaths the entire estate to
his brothers and sisters. The Hobson peo
ple have announced that they will con
test to the bitter end the threat of the
child's mother to secure the fortune for
her offspring. They will also charge
fraud, alleging- that Georgia is an adopted
child and that Georg-e H. Hobson is not
its father.
The will bequeaths $125,000 to be divided
equally between Mrs. Lizzie Anderson;
Hobson's sister, and his four brothers, all
of Pueblo. The wife's only benefit is his
life insurance, amounting to $3000. The will
was filed for probate November 19 last,
just a month after the testator's death.
In February last Mrs. Hobson filed in
court a statement that she was about to
become a mother and asked for an order
securing for her child a share in her hus
band's estate. Mrs. Anderson tried to se
cure a court order allowing physicians to
ascertain the truthfulness or falsity of
Mrs. Hobson's statement, but the court
denied the motion. Mrs. Hobson says she
will have no trouble in proving that the
child is her legitimate offspring. She is
now living with her parents at 1014 Bonny
Brae avenue, Los Angeles, where Georgia
was born.

"W., WASHINGTON, April 22.â Secretary
Hay has given Carlos Martinez Sllva", the
Colombian Minister, to understand that
he cannot negotiate & treaty with him
for the acquisition" by; the United States
of the Panama canal until *Congress de
termines upon the route to be construct
ed. Not even a preliminary agreement;
such as was signed with Nicaragua and
Costa Rica, will be negotiated v Dr. SUva
will, however, remain in Washington dia
cusslnK various questions in connection
with the Panama canal 90 that in case,
Congress Authorises the President to ac
quire this route, the treaty may be Imme
diately concluded. . l .
Dr. 8UvÂ« haa ftmnd. that an important
change tn the sentiment of this country
with respect to the canal has occurred.
"The only argument against the Pan
ama osuml umdo in the preliminary report
of the isthmian Canal Commission," he
eaJd to-day, "In that an agreement with
the Panama Canal Company tor Ameri
can acquisition of the route is imprac
ticable. Since the preliminary report con
taining this declaration was made, Colom
bia, through Its legation. tn Washington,
has submitted to the Secretary of State
a formal memomndum embodying the
conditions upon whtch it is willing that
the United States should acquire the
canal. These conditions are highly ad
vantageous to the American Government.
"Mi Hutin, president of Panama Canal
Company, who has been unable to sell
the company's rights without Colombia's
consent, has assured me that his com
pany is willing to dispose of its conces
sions on reasonable terms. I think the
company will be willing to abide by the
result of an appraisal." ,
"The Isthmian Canal Commission, in its
preliminary report, said that the Panama
Canal was shorter and could be built for
$53,000,000 less, than the Nicaragua vMite,
excluding the 'cost of the concession. All
technical problems have been solved at
Panama, but they, have still to be de
termined at Nicaragua, The sanitary
conditions hare greatly improved at
Panama and there will be no such exces
sive death rate as would prevail at
Nicaragua. There j are excellent harbors
at Colon, and \ Panama and there are no
harbors on the Nicaragua route. I think
every engineer fully understands, the pre
pondering advantage of the Panama over
the Nicaragua route."

COMMISSION WANTS PAY
OF COPYISTS WITHHELD
Scrves Notice on Treasurer That Re
corder Godchaux Has Violated
Charter.
The Civil Service Commission addressed
a communication to the Treasurer yester
day, requesting that official not to pay
the salary demands of the four copyists,
who were recently appointed by Recorder
Godchaux. The reason assigned by the
commission is that the appointments were
made in violation of the civil service pro
visions of the charter. The Auditor has
refused to comply with a similar request.
In its communication the commission
says:
The charter confers xipon the Treasurer dis
cretionary powers in the payment of moneys
out of the treasury. Section 2, chapter III,
article IV of the charter provides that the
Treasurer shell not "pay out any part of such
moneys, nor allow the same to pass out of
Ms personal custody, except upon demands
authorized by law or this charter." Section
6 uf the same chapter and article adds: "But
the allowance or approval of the Auditor, or of
the Supervisory, or of any" department., board
or officer, of any demand which is not au
thorized by law or this charter, shall afford
no warrant to tho Treasurer for paying the
same."
The advice of the Auditor's attorney that
the Civil Service Commission shall "pursue the
remedy" cannot relieve the Auditor and the
Treasurer from their clear duty to decline to
audit or pay a demand against the treasury
that is susceptible of grave legal doubt.

Edward Eisner, the dramatlzer of Olga
Nethersole's production of "Under Two
Flags," is sure of a tropical interview with
actor James Neil when they meet. From
present Indications it looks as if the
meeting of the two men will be far more
dramatic than the tent scene in the play
when hero Bertie Cecil Royellieu and Cap
tain Chateauroy. alias "The Black
Hawk," fight with long swords and cut
big gashes In the atmosphere.
Neil" arrived in town yesterday with his
company, which opens at the California
Theater next Monday night. In his reper
toir.o is Edward Eisner's version of
Ouida's famous novel, of which Neil
claims to have the coast rights and which
he intended producing during his engage
ment in this city. He was surprised to
learn that the drama was being played
at tho Grand, Opera-house and upon a
visit to the theater he found that Eisner
had, besides renting the play tp him, also
leased it to "William R. Wilson, a theat
rical man. Wilson had sublet the play
for one week to Morosco, with the under
standing that he wouid buy all the scenic
effects, which Wilson would use on the.
road with a company that he is forming
In this city. " .
Neil last night said that he did not
blame either Wilson or Morosco for their
share in the transaction, but he spoke of
Klsner in very severe terms. Neil says
that he produced the play in Buffalo,
Seattle and Portland, Or., and did a big
business with it. -He says that he will
not produce it in this city though he
originally intended to and that he will
call Eisner to account for his action in
renting the play to two people when he
meets him.
Nell also made a contract with Miss
Alice Kausen of New York and Clarence
Brune for the production of Fanny
Devenport's old plays, "Fedora" and "La
Tosca."Â» Nell saya that when he rented
the plays he was shown a contract which
was signed by Melbourne McDowell in
which McDowell relinquished all rights to
the plays. It now appears that McDowell
will shortly appear at the Grand Opera
house in both "Fedora" and "La Tosca"
and he claims that he made no contract
with anybody authorizing the production
of the plays.

" No objection was made to stuffing the
ballot-box. ! v In fact, the managers wel
comed this plan as one that would bring
the largest returns to them, and they
were not disappointed in -their expecta
tions.
-A prize was also, offered for the most
popular young lady In the eyes of the
German colony. Everybody was drawn
into the Â¦ two contests, and when the
judges Â¦ declared the results the victors
were made to appreciate the honors of
such distinction.
From the start. Miss F. M. Hays led the
procession - in Â¦ the contest to be- voted the
most beautiful young lady. While many
others disputed with her for the prize
there were none who could come up to the
standard set by the judges, and Miss Hays
triumphantly walked off with the prize.
In the contest for the 1 most popular
young lady a great latitude was afforded.
Miss Anna Gerichten carried off this prize
with such a decided majority that even
her rivals were constrained to willingly
relinquish to her the palms of victory.
Pitted against her were many charming
young ladies, who, although defeated,
frankly admitted that no better choice
could have been made.
In the shooting contest, ten prizes were
distributed among the most expert marks
men, the leaders of the Verein Eintracht
Schuetzen section being mainly distin
guished for their marksmanship. Valua
ble prizes' were given to each of the lead
ing contestants in the various shooting
classes. The winners in the order of pre
cedence were: H. Bender, H. Stettin, W.
Harker, H. Kuhls, Captain F. G. Kuhls,
C. Auer, L. Streit, O. Ludwlg, D. J. Han
sen, R. Liebenscheid.

REIN EINTRACHT FAIR. -
Many a successful man begins at the
top of the ladder and goes downâ when he
has business in the cellar.
Mining and financial circles were aeos
with excitement yesterday over the newj
published In The Call that Henry Brat
nober, the well-known and popular mining
expert and capitalist, had sailed from.
London for home after clearing up more
than five million dollars In the modern
Babylon.
When the Klondike excitement was at
its height Bratnober went to the Arctic
region for the Rothschilds, backed by un
limited capital. He â¢ became acquainted
in Dawson with Jack Dalton, the man who
founded the famous Dalton trail, and the
; two became fast friends.
On returning from Dawson Bratnober
severed his connection with the Roths
childs and branched out for himself. He
, associated himself with the late Thomas
Mein, A- Wartenweiler, J. H. Sayre and
others who are prominent in nr.an.cial
, circles.
One year ago Bratnober revisited tha
Klondike and returned to this city with
Jack Dalton. The two started for Lon
don just before Christmas and Bratnober
at the time stated he was "out for bis
game." ~
It Is understood that Henry Callahan, a
mining expert, is the man, who gave cer
tain information to Bratnober. that the
latter used to great advantage.
Whitaker Wright Remembered.
Hundreds 1 of people in this State have
reason to remember Whitaker Wright,
who visited California about twenty years
ago, ifnd was the head of many disastrous
mining ventures in Lake County. Wright
finally dropped from sight and went to
London, where he ingratiated himself
with members, of the aristocracy, mem
bers of Parliament and other prominent
men. He then went to Australia and
started the "Lake View Syndicate" of
mines and the British public swallowed
the bait with avidity. Money fairly
poured into the coffers of Wright and his
associates and the arch-promoter became
a financial pillar in London. He secured
a footing in the "London and Globe Syn
dicate," a solid concern, and sold the di
rectors considerable stock of the "Lake
View" mines In Australia.
Henry Callahan. a mining expert, was
sent from London to Australia by the
London and Globe Company to report on
the Lake View mines, and he left Aus
tralia for home & few weeks ago, passing
; through San Francisco on his return trip.
Bratnober became acquainted with Calla
han when he passed through this city
en route to Australia and it is generally
understood that Callahan gave Bratnober
a tip to "watch Lake View stock."
Stock Bulled and Beared.
When Bratnober went to London last
winter he "beared" the stock and secured
a large number of shares. "When the
market was "bulled" by favorable reports
from Australia, the London and Glove
Syndicate eagerly bought up all the stock
it could lay its hands on and Bratnober
and his friends are reported to have
cleared up by the transaction more than
five million dollars.
Bratnober sailed from England on Sat
urday on the Lucania and his friends are
eagerly awaiting his return to this city.
A. "Wartenweiler. who shares offices in
the Crocker building- with Bratnober, de
clined to give any Information as to the
big deals made in London by Bratnober.
"I know that he made money during the
winter," said Wartenweiler in speaking 1 of
Bratnober, "but I have no information as
to this last transaction. I had a cable
from him a week ago saying: he was com
ing home."
Mrs. Bratnober moved from the Palace
Hotel to Piedmont when her husband
went to Europe and lives in a palatial
home in the suburban city. "When Brat
nober arrives in San Francisco he is
bound to receive a hearty welcome and
details of his good fortune are eagerly
awaited. ".

Alcazar.
The Alcazar has a good thing this week
In Paul M. Potter's four-act play. "The
The Bcenery was beautiful andâ I was
about to cay "appropriate," but I should
eay. "inuch too good for the lines."
L. DU-PONT SYLB. '
The ladies and gentlemen engaged in
this extraordinary farrago are entitled to
our respectful sympathy. Mr. Webster
and Miss Stone, as the most intelligent,
must have suffered the most. They have
each an excellent stage presence, a well
trained voice and a quiet strength of man
ner which show that if only they were
riven a play they could not fail to interest
and to move us.
The character of Cigarette Is that of a
vivandiere whose constant talk is of broil
and battle, who thirsts for the blood of
Arabs defending their country against in-*
vading tyrants, who smokes and swears
and drinks like the veriest jack-trooper
of them all. This tigress-in-boots has a
very pretty affection for the hero, who
evidently does not "know the difference
between life and rhetoric." This affection
works itself out in a series of incidents
that require the assistance of horses, re
volvers, sabres, thunder, lightning and
dust storms to make them go off. And
there's your play! A troglodyte would
weep at having to sit through it and two
performances would shatter the constitu
tion of a cave-man.
Throughout the remaining acts the au
thor endeavors to carry your sympathy
for a hero who, in this aforesaid first act,
has committed an absurd and altogether
unnecessary piece of self-sacrifice. This
makes the sentiment of the entire play
ring false; instead of pitying your hero
and sympathizing with him you feel only
& languid contempt for a man who has
richly deserved every misfortune that has
fallen upon him.
IT IS really sad to contemplate the
amount of human effort that goes
into such a production as "Under Two
Flags" (Morosco's) and then to real
ize how utterly wasted has been the
greater part of this effort. To begin with,
it must have required a considerable,
combined exertion on the part of Ouida,
the ungrammatical, and her tedious
adapter. M. Eisner, to unite an act as in
geniously bad as the first act presented
last night; such dummy characters, such
clumsy construction, such tawdry senti
ment! Xever, burely, was there a compli
cation brought about by such mechanical
devices, with such an absolute disregard
of probable motives, with such childish
assurance that the audience would accept
anything the author chose to throw at
them.

Weekly Ministers' Meetings.
; Rev. J., N. Beard- discussed the use of
individual communion cups at the weekly
meeting of the Methodist ministers yes
terday: 'He, informed his brethren that
he had inaugurated their use in his church
and had found it a success. A delegatiion
of ministers from the Presbyterian Min
isters' Union, composed of Drs.tBell, Be
vier and Willard, attended the meeting
and asked that a committee be. appointed
to co-operate with the committees trom
other denominational bodies to arrange
for a final evangelical movement on the
first' of Â¦ the, year under the leadership of
J. Wilbur Chapman, the famous evan
gelist. -Rev. John Stephens, Rev. J. N.
Beard and Rev. . S. D. Hutsinpiller were
appointed a committee to aid the project.
Rev. F.K. Baker was appointed, a com
mittee of one -to 'draft: suitable resolutions
thanking the Federal authorities for their
excellent work in endeavoring to curtail
the slave traffic in Chinatown. Rev. R.
H. Fink read an interesting paper before*
the Presbyterian Ministers' . Union on
"Why People do not Attend Church on
Sunday." . ,' . .. Â¦

French family. The Prussian offlcets in
vite a crowd of Parisian dancing girls to
the castle, and during the subsequent fes
tivities an insulting toast is proposed 'by.
one of the officers, one Lieutenant ' Eric
von Rodeck, that is overheard by Yvonne,
Fister of the Baron de Grand Pre. Yvonne
dashes a glass of wine in the officer's face
as the retort courteous and he swears to
be revenged.
The opportunity for revenge presents it
self in the second act, when, finding
Yvonne in a country inn, where she has
gone to meet her soldier brother, he de
termines to ruin her. She successfully
appeals to his chivalry, however, and he
leaves her. only for her to fall into worse
hands in the person of the landlord of the
inn, Bobeche. Yvonne, in the dark, im
agines that it is the lieutenant, repentant

The shooting of the horses of the Ninth
Cavalry found suffering from glanders
still continues at the Presidio. ; Forty
have. been killed since the arrival ( of the
animals at the post eight days ago, and
to-day twenty-five more will be released
from pain by bullets from army revolv
ers. Despite the precautions taken to pre
vent the spread of the glanders the dis
ease seems to spread daily. -,-
Private Ogletree of the Eighteenth In
fantry was shot through the left arm,yes
terday morning by the accidental dis
charge of his rifle. The bullet fractured
the bone in the forearm in two places. â¢
Word was received yesterday at army
headquarters that the transport Sheridan
had left Manila with the Forty-fifth and
Forty-sixth regiments on board. -

M. Gaston Deschamps of Paris, literary
critic of "Le Temps", and one of the most
distinguished" of -present-day French men
of letters, will arrive in San Francisco at
6:45 'Friday evening. Throughout the fol
lowing week M. Deschamps will lecture at
the University of California on "The Con
temnorary Stage." These lectures, to
wWch the public is invited, will bo -given
in the lecture room of the Students , Ob
servatory at 4 o clock every afternoon
from Monday to Saturday, April 29 to May
4 inclusive. M. Deschamps will speak in
French. -The detailed programme will be
"Monday. ''April 29. "The Masters or the
Contemporary Stage"; Tuesday. April 30.
"The Marriage Question" ; Wednesday ,
May 1. "Family Life"; Thursday, May 2,
"The Social Question"; Friday, May 3.
"The Heroic Drama"; Saturday, May 4,
conclusion, "The Future of the Stage in
M. n Deschamps was born at Nelle. Deux
Sevres in 1861. He studied at the College
of Niort, then at the College Salnte Barbe.
in Paris and was admitted in 1882 at the
Upper Normal School. Received as a fel
low of the university- in. 1885, he was se
lected by competition â¢ to be sent to the
French Archaeological School in Athens.
It is as a pensionnalre of that school
that M. Gaston Deschampa explored sev
eral regions of Asia Minor, and his epi
graphic work made the subject of several
reports to the Academy of Inscriptions
and Belles Lettres. In the Intervals of his
trip M. Deschamps acted as literary. and
political correspondent of Le Journal ;des
Debats for Athens. In 1890 he was man
aging edlt6r of the Journal des, Debats
and contributed also to the Revue de Deux
Mondes and the Revue Bleue. .-. He pub
lished in 1892 "La Grece d'Aujourdhui,
which was "couronne" . by the French
Academy.' The following year he published
"Sur les Routes d'Asic," souvenirs of
travel. He then succeeded Anatole France
as the literary critic of the Temps, and for
the last seven years he has been. writing
a weekly . "feuilleton" on contemporary
literary, movements. - These essays have
been reunited in "La Vie et Les Livres,
which count already five volumes. .

Friday to negotiate an oil deal, but he
did not do anything in the" matter, and
when he failed to make his appearance
Saturday, Dannmeyer became suspicious.
He made inquiry and learned that Moi
sant had called upon his wife, who is
living in this city with her mother, and
had asked as a favor to be allowed to
take' their child to the Orpheum for the
matinee. The husband and wife have been
living apart for some years.; Moisant did
not return* with the child.
Dannmeyer notified Captain Seymour
yesterday morning, and it was soon dis
covered that Moisant had left on Satur
day evening's train for El Paso en routÂ©
for Mexico, and it is presumed that he
has the child with him.

â¢ An- hour after O'Callaghan was seen he

dispatch from New Mexico, but did not

sant, and Dannemeyer is as. silent as his

whether Mrs. Moisant will charge her
husband with kidnaping her child. The
lady did not visit police headquarters and
the detectives .were unable to locate her
last night. : . \
The Police Department is of the opinion
that Dannemeyer and Moisant have had
a family and business quarrel and that
the- charge of felony embezzlement has
but little foundation.
